"Of the extraction of the vein of the thigh."
"This vein is generated in the chambers in tawny lands such as Al-Aqaba in Arabic lands and in dry lands of India with little tenderness where there is neither grass nor herb."
"And it is also generated in other parts of the body besides the chambers."
"Thus, the condition of this one is due to putrefaction that comes from leaving the intestines as if they were venomous serpents and worms generating between the intestines and the flesh."
"The sign of the onset of this condition is from this vein that appears in the inflamed chamber; and near the location it is congested, and near there begins the vein to emerge from the site of that congestion which resembles a plant root."
"Then, when the extremity appears, at that time you should wrap around it one piece of lead weighing as much as two dragons, and then tie it and leave the lead suspended from the chamber."
"So many times when something goes outwards due to this condition, you should wrap that in lead and tie it up." "And if it does not prolong itself, then you should cut off a part of it and wrap the remaining portion; but do not cut it from the root, for if you cut that it will contract and enter into the flesh and cause an abscess and putrefaction in the location with a bad ulceration."
"So that it may be drawn out gradually and extracted little by little, until the last of it is pulled out and none of it remains in the body."
"And from this vein, some men have said that it is as long as two and twenty palms, but I have seen one which was thirty."
"And if it is cut in the time of its treatment, then insert a tube into the wound and pierce that with a long tube into the body until all that is in the matter is evacuated, and apply putrefying medicines to the location for some days; and afterwards treat it with the cure for apostumes."
"And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the junction of the foot or in the foot itself, and comes from many openings; and it is said that each opening sends forth one branch."
"And thus treat it as we have said in the division above, with what has been mentioned previously."
"Concerning cutting for worms generated under the skin, and is called bovine illness."
"This illness is called in some lands 'bovine disease' because often it occurs in oxen and there is one worm which is born between the backbone and the flesh, and it goes throughout the body pushing and pulling itself along until it tears apart the backbone and comes out."
"And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humors, just as happens with worms, serpents, earthworms, and roundworms in the belly."
"And it is feared on account of its malice, for when it goes into the body and rises up to the head, and reaches the brain, then often it breaks open there and enters in and destroys the brain, which happens many times."
"And therefore when you wish to treat it and extract it, first determine its course and appearance by feeling. Then constrain with something that is above it and something that is below it with pressure and binding, and then dry around it and extract it."
"And if it is caught in the flesh and not removed, then apply a caustic with fire to the location where it resides until it dies. The greatest cause that is feared is corruption of it, by which it corrupts the location as has been said."
"But if you wish that one who has come near the head to be removed from there, then constrain over the eyebrow with strong binding, and dry around it, and extract it. And let the patient use medicines to purify his body by evacuating bad putrid humors, and avoid foods that cause putrefaction."
"Concerning cutting for the illness called 'nafir'."
"This illness is called in our land 'nafir', and it is pain that comes to certain limbs, and also moves from limb to limb."
"And I have already seen that one as I will tell you about it." "I was summoned to a sick woman in a village. Upon exposing her arm, I saw a small inflammation on the vein of the forearm. After an hour had passed, I observed that inflammation spreading thus: it appeared as if it were alive and moved from place to place, but more slowly than porphyry."
"Then the pain went away from that spot and fixed itself in the muscle. It stayed there for about an hour."
"And then it moved to the other side of her body, reaching her other arm."
"And I saw that it was moving around her entire body, changing places rapidly from member to member. It was truly remarkable." "I had seen something like this before in my practice, though I had rarely heard of pain being transferred from member to member." "But I have never seen anything like this in the hospitals as I did with this woman."
"And I thought that it was because the woman was elderly, with dry skin and visible veins, which made the movement of this wind-like sensation more apparent."
"And it is necessary that this condition does not appear in those who are delicate and have humid bodies with hidden veins."
"And when you wish to treat it, if the patient feels pain, then after an hour or two has passed without any apparent sign at the hospital as we have said, then press upon that spot lightly and gently until the restricted wind-like sensation has been released, and cauterize the location."
"But if you do not see it at your hospitals, then treat it with exercise of the body and whatever expels wind. Use pills made from fetid herbs and pills containing serpentaria and similar substances."
"On arrow extraction."
"Arrows are varied according to species and according to the parts of the body where they strike. Their variety is such that some have large points, others small, concave, or blunt, which are solid. Others have three edges, while yet others have tongues; still others are star-shaped."
"And signs of brain injury include when struck by an arrow which pierces the bone and exits through the fontanel above the brain. This causes sudden loss of consciousness, darkness of vision, vertigo, heat in the eyes, inflammation and heat of the tongue, spasms and confusion of intellect, vomiting of bile, difficulty speaking and loss of voice, and there appears from the wound white fluid like dust, and watery discharge like the moisture of flesh."
"And therefore, if such signs appear to you, then you should abstain from treating and removing the arrow unless it is outside."
"But signs of an arrow having struck the heart include: it is near the left nipple and feels firm where it has lodged, not hollow; there is a strong sensation like the beating of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound. Death ensues suddenly without delay."
"And signs that an arrow has struck the lung include: frothy blood issues from the wound, vessels near the throat are suffocated, and the patient's complexion changes. He exhales much breath rapidly or sighs for air."
"And if the arrow strikes the pleura which is in the chest, there is pain near the little ribs. Breathing becomes difficult with violent pain and sighing, and the muscles of all members are not moved."
"And if the arrow strikes the stomach, thereupon much undigested food issues from the wound."
"And if the arrow strikes the abdomen and becomes lodged there, thereupon some opening of the wound appears, or the skin is torn, or an intestine is ruptured. There is no hope in treating the removal of the arrow."
"And if the arrow strikes the bladder and urine is expelled from outside, and thereupon some part of the wound appears externally. There is strong pain over the wound, therefore know that it is mortal."
"But the other members such as face, neck, throat, shoulders, arms, shoulder blades, spine, collarbone, hip bone, and leg, and their counterparts of the limbs are saved according to their natural positions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not poisoned."
"And I will tell you what I have seen concerning these arrows, so that you may know how to treat them."
"It happened that an arrow struck the tear duct of the eye in one person's nose. I removed it from another opening near his ear, and he was healed without damage to his eye."
"And I removed another arrow from a Jew which had struck him in the socket of his eye. I left the eyelid closed and then opened it gradually until at last the arrow was exposed, but only a small part of its tip remained attached to its shaft. It was one of those sharp arrows used by the Turks, with a lion-head point and without feathers."
"And the Jew recovered, suffering no damage to his eye."
I extracted another arrow from a man named Crestian, which had fallen into his thigh, an Arab arrow it was, and it's that one with its ears; then I incised him between two tendons, for it was submerged in his thigh; then I gently removed that one, and Crestian is healed.
I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought he would die from it; and since thirty days have passed and there is no change in his condition, I incised over the arrow and removed it, and he is healed, and no bad accident happened to him.
I saw another man in whom an arrow had stuck in his back, and the wound was consolidated there; since it was necessary to remove the arrow from the middle of the flesh.
I saw a woman in whom an arrow had stuck in her belly, and the wound was consolidated inside her, and she did not change in any of her bodily functions, nor did she suffer harm from it in any way.
I saw another man in whom an arrow had stuck in his face and the wound was consolidated but the arrowhead remained, without causing much pain, and many others appeared like these.
I extracted the arrow from one of the king's princes who had it stuck in the middle of his nose and was already inclining towards the right side a little, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to treat him about three days after the arrow had entered.
And then I found the wound of the arrow very narrow; and then I probed it carefully with a probe, but did not find anything; but he felt pricking from behind the right ear, so I thought that that pricking was caused by the point of the arrow.
And I widened the place with an emollient plaster which has attractive and digestive virtues, being confident that the place would be opened up so that I could see the place where the arrow was, in order to extract it.
And there did not happen in the place any accidental sign that the arrow had reached that place; and I continued with the plaster on it for many days, and nothing accidental happened. And the wound became consolidated meanwhile, and the illness from extracting it lasted for many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me about it.
And then I put on the wound an acute corrosive medicine for many days until it was opened up, and with a probe I explored there and found the point of the arrow subtly adhered to the shaft.
And immediately afterwards I adjusted the application of that acute medicine on the wound until the point of the arrow appeared visibly to me, and it took me more than four months to do this.
And when the wound was enlarged enough so that I could introduce forceps into it, and did not close up again afterwards, then I did not stop probing and devising about it with various methods of engineering until one day I began to extract it using forceps made specifically for this purpose, as described at the end of this chapter, until I extracted it; and immediately afterwards I dressed that wound.
And some doctors judged that the cartilage of the nose could not be restored, but I restored it and the wound became consolidated, and the illness was completely healed without any remainder.
And I tell you the quality of extracting some arrows, so that you may understand better what I have not described here, for the parts of this art and the definitions of it are not understood through narration nor can they be comprehended from this book.
And the wise craftsman compares little with much; and about what is present he speaks more than about what is absent; and he finds new operation and new instrument according to the descent of foreign causes of the art when they happen.
And therefore I say that arrows are not thrown out of the members in which they are stuck except by two means: either by attraction of the place where it entered, or by the opposite part.
And that which is thrown out by the place where it entered, either is an arrow proceeding in a fleshy location, and is attracted and expelled.
And if it does not happen immediately upon being struck, then you should leave it for some days until the flesh that is around it swells up and the attraction of it and its exit are alleviated.
Similarly, if it is stuck in bone and does not come out immediately, then leave it for some days and stir it daily with attraction and motion, and soon it will be expelled.
And if it does not come out even after several days, then you should bore around the arrow in that bone with a subtle awl until the location is widened by the arrow, and then extract it.
But if the arrow is already hidden in the location of the body and is hidden from sensation, then you should search for it with a probe, and if you find it, then attract it out with some instruments which are suitable for its attraction.
And if you cannot reach it because of the contraction of the wound and the length of the point, and here there is neither bone nor nerve nor vein, then you should operate on the wound until it is widened and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
And if it is held by the ears with which it is gripped, then you should loosen the flesh in which it is stuck on all sides with whatever engineering you can devise; and devise how to achieve this, if not by means of the escape of the flesh through the fracture of the ears or twisting of them until it comes out.
And when you are administering the extraction of the arrow in any location, then use your hand twisted with forceps on all sides so that it can achieve its objective; and be very subtle about this so that the arrow is not broken and it is done easily for you both in attracting it out and extracting it.
And if you cannot reach it at that time, then leave it for some days until the parts around it have recovered, and afterwards try again; for now it will be easier. But if you experience counterflow of blood, then administer what we have said about treatment in its chapter.
And guard your power of incision of vein or nerve or cord, and administer whatever engineering you can think of for the escape of the arrow; and do this with facility and calmness and slowness and steadfastness as I and you have recommended.
And ensure that you compress the arrow so that the illness follows its natural course according to the figure which the arrow was supposed to follow in it, for this is more beneficial. "But if this is not possible for you, then you shall perform what is possible according to your knowledge."
"But the arrows that are shot from the other side of the target, or those for which no partition has yet begun to be made in the partitions of the other side, or those whose extreme tip is felt in the partition above the nearest prop, you shall cut across it; and make your section according to the quantity in which the forceps take hold; and then throw that one away, as it is too light to use."
"But if it is retained in the bone, then you shall twist the arrow according to its direction of entry into the bone, and extract it little by little, bit by bit."
"And if not, leave that one alone for a few days, and then remove it when it has begun to rot."
"And if the shaft of the arrow is in there too, then you shall pull it out with it."
"But if the shaft has broken off and you wish to administer traction, then insert a probe there, if it is not too short; curve it concavewards so that its concavity fits over the end of the arrow, and then pull it out."
"But if the arrow is concave, then you shall pull it out with a probe entering into that curve, for the arrow will be lightened by that."
"And if the arrowhead is barbed, then you must cut away all the flesh in which it is embedded, if that be possible for you; and then dress the wound with whatever seems suitable."
"And if the arrow has entered the bone, or the belly, or the bladder, or the flank, and is close enough for you to touch it with your hand, and it is possible for you to cut upon it, then cut thereupon and guard against injury to a vein or nerve; extract the arrow and sew up the wound if necessary, and dress it until healed."
"Forms of forceps with which arrows are extracted:"
"The ends of them are shaped like the beak of a bird, and they have teeth like a saw when they grasp the arrow, so that it is not allowed to slip away."
"And they come in many different shapes, large and small, few or numerous according to the size of the arrowhead and its thickness, and the width of the wound and the closeness of its contraction."
"like a hook, to facilitate entry into the wound in a concave arrow and its extraction with it."
"On phlebotomy of veins:"
"The veins commonly used for phlebotomy are thirty in number, of which sixteen are in the head. These include two pulsating veins near the ears, called helixes; two herbs that appear on both temples; two veins located in the lacrimal ducts of both eyes, called aspiciences; a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein at the tip of the nose; two jugulars at the neck; two veins on the upper lip; two veins on the lower lip; two veins that are called algels; and two veins below the tongue." "But the veins that are commonly opened in the arms are five in number: of these one is cephalic, and it lies on the outer side; the people call it 'the vein of the head'. Another is median, composed of a branch of the basilica and another of the cephalic; the people name it 'vein of the whole body'. The basilica is that vein which is situated on the inner side of the arm, and is called 'cubital' by the vulgar. The funis of the arm lies upon the forearm, and this is where phlebotomy takes place when it manifests itself above the elbow. And the salvatella is the vein that lies between the little finger and the one next to it."
"And in the leg and thigh there are three veins: one of these lies below the curvature of the knee on the outer side. The second is the saphenous vein, whose location is behind the thigh on the inner side. The sciatic vein lies behind the thigh on the outer side. And there are other veins similar to these in the thigh."
"The use of the veins near the ears is for ancient catarrhs, migraine, sciatica and ulcers in the head from old injuries."
"And the manner of opening those veins is as follows: first, shave the head of the patient and rub it well with a soft cloth on both sides where the veins are located. Then place a firm bandage around the neck of the patient so that two places appear near the ears in two depressed areas of the head. Find the vein with your index finger and feel its pulse with your finger, then make the incision there."
"Then take the lancet scalpel, which is inserted, and introduce it below the vein in the elbow so that phlebotomy reaches the bone." "Then raise your hand with the vein and elbow above, and make an incision in the vein with an incising cut." "And let the length of the incision be about two fingers or thereabouts, and let blood flow out as much as you wish."
"Then press with cotton and plaster to stop it from bleeding until it is healed."
"And it has already been mentioned at the beginning of this book how to perform incisions on these veins and how to cauterize them."
"But of the two arterial veins that are in the temples, phlebotomy is useful for ancient migraine, chronic headache, habitual ophthalmia, and acute effusions of blood from the eyes."
"And the manner of phlebotomy on those veins is as follows: tie a cloth tightly around the patient's neck until both veins appear prominently and their pulsation can be felt with your finger. Then make the incision there."
"And then raise the elbow above the vein with your index finger, introduce the phlebotomy scalpel beneath it in an incising manner and elevate the vein upwards. Then make the incision as you would on other veins, allowing blood to flow according to your needs."
"And then untie the bandage from the patient and place your finger on the vein for an hour. Then apply cotton and powder to it and press firmly with a tight bandage until it is healed."
"And their memorization and incision, as well as those of the other veins mentioned earlier in this book, have already been described."
"And the use of phlebotomy on the frontal vein is especially useful for ancient diseases of the face, migraine, redness, and foul ulcers."
"And the manner of phlebotomy on that vein is as follows: have the patient tie a bandage around their neck so that the vein appears. Then take the instrument called the 'fossor', which has this shape:"
"Place the point of the 'fossor' on that vein and pierce it with a quick strike or other cause in the manner shown, allowing blood to flow according to your desire. Then untie the perforation from the patient and press it firmly until it is healed."
"Sometimes it is opened with a lat flebotomy, but it need not be of acute point like other flebotomies; rather, it should be on the blunt end, for if sharp, it might break against the bone when subtle."
"The junction of both veins which are near the tear ducts of two eyes for scabies of the eyelids due to heat and grease in the eyes, and for facial diseases."
"The quality of the flebotomy is such that the patient should hold their head steady with a bandage, and carefully perform the flebotomy thereupon, standing above the patient's head."
"And the flebotomy should be performed horizontally for about half an inch with a thin flebotomy needle, as there is little flesh in this area. If the flebotomy needle is too sharp, it might break."
"And carefully make the blood flow as much as necessary, and pause thereupon, pressing with that until the next day, and then remove the bandage."
"But the flebotomy of the nasal vein is beneficial for acute fever and sudden sneezing, and for facial diseases, migraine, and heat that occurs in the face, especially when it is chronic."
"And the quality of this flebotomy is that the patient should hold their head steady and place their nose on their left hand, taking a thin flebotomy needle and inserting it into the middle of the forehead vertically, as the vein does not appear palpable here, and blood will begin to flow soon."
"Then slowly withdraw your hand along with the flebotomy needle and make the blood flow according to your needs; then bind it for one night, as it heals quickly."
"But the benefit of flebotomy on both cheeks is to relieve pain and congestion, and to prevent or treat leprosy, eczema, itching, and corrosive ulcers like herpes."
"And the quality of this flebotomy is that each patient should hold their head steady with their hands; and the operator should stand above the patient's head, while the patient sits on a stool."
"And then carefully flebotomize the vein on the long side with a large flebotomy needle little by little; then draw blood according to moderation or as much as necessary, and do the same for the other vein."
"And then untie the bandage and press both veins with a moderate compression to prevent the patient from fainting. Let him rest for the night, as the wound will be healed by morning."
"And the benefit of flebotomy on the veins at the elbows is to treat small abscesses and pimples in the mouth, gingivitis, ulcers, fissures, and ulcers around the nose and its surroundings."
"And the quality of this flebotomy is that you should make the patient sit in front of you and hold their head steady with a cloth. Then gently turn their lips and observe the two veins, one on the right lip and the other on the left; their demonstration is through their darkness, and especially where there are small dark veins around them. Then incise each one with an oblique incision."
"But if there is doubt and you do not know which one it is, then make the incision on the larger one of those two, or where there is evident pus."
"And do the same for both veins that are above the lips."
"But the benefit of flebotomy on both veins under the tongue is to treat fainting spells that occur in the throat, uvitis, and oral diseases."
"And the quality of flebotomizing those veins is that you should stand behind the patient facing the sunlight, raise their tongue, and observe it from below. On one side there is a vein and on the other side another vein, both tending towards darkness in color."
"And then flebotomize each one; but be careful not to go too deep with your hand while flebotomizing those veins, as beneath them are arteries."
"And the veins that are commonly flebotomized in the elbow are those that are present in all men. Flebotomy of these is performed in two ways: either with a flebotomy needle dipped in myrtle or olive oil, subtly inclined; or cutting with a flebotomic scalpel called 'alnessil,' and this is its form:"
"The side of it should be like this: it is convenient for opening concave veins filled with blood, especially those appearing thick and containing turbid blood."
"And this flebotomy needle is of lesser width and has a very subtle tip. It is convenient for flebotomizing small veins containing clear blood."
"But the benefit of flebotomizing the basilica vein, which is one of three veins, is that it draws blood from diseases that are below the throat and neck, as well as from sciatica and abdominal pain."
"And it is convenient for the flebotomist, when flebotomizing that vein, to carefully observe and be cautious in it, as there is an artery below it. If they err and strike with the flebotomy needle, they will cut that artery and cause bleeding. Therefore, it is not advisable to flebotomize it with a pointed flebotomy needle, but rather cut with alnessil."
"And if the basilica vein does not appear through manifest appearance, then you should leave it and move to another one, or seek some of its branches; or flebotomize in place of it at the end of the arm where it is manifest. And cut it with alnessil flebotomy as we have said."
"But if you want to flebotomize the median vein, then it is necessary in front of the tension of the arm that you touch the place within which you know the location of the pulse. Then sign over it with your thumb; and bend the arm and cut the vein with a concave section using alnessil flebotomy as we have said. And be careful not to strike the artery some distance away from its location."
"And when you wish, as you tighten the ligature, press in the place where you have marked with your thumb. Then that swelling is swelling of the artery, and therefore avoid it."
"And if blood flows like a child's urine during flebotomy and the blood is red, then know that it is arterial blood. Then lift your finger from there for some distance; remove your finger, and if the bleeding stops, as often happens, then tighten the arm and leave him."
"And protect the patient from mucus of that wound. And be cautious with it for several days until it is healed to prevent infection."
"But if the bleeding does not stop and you feel that it is necessary to use a medicine for this purpose, then you have cut the artery. If it appears to you, as the end of it is contracted and the blood stops. Then take a fistful of cotton and place it there; then take some mustard seed and plaster it on; and wrap it with bandages and powder for another day."
"And if blood is cut off, it is good; and otherwise, then treat that with which the preceding was remembered to absorb the flow of blood."
"And cutting off the blood from it is difficult in many positions due to the small space of the wound and the possibility of hitting the arm's ligament."
"However, phlebotomy of the black vein is beneficial for drawing blood from the upper part of the head and from below the chest; for this reason it consists of branches of the basilic vein and branches of the cephalic vein, as we have said."
"And therefore one should be especially afraid during the phlebotomy of that [vein], because underneath it is a nerve; and therefore if one adjusts in the puncture of the phlebotomy and touches the nerve, stupor will occur, and its healing is difficult, and often it is not healed entirely. And this nerve can be seen by sensation many times, but if it is hidden from some people and is subtle and does not appear, then you should pause the phlebotomy with which you are performing the phlebotomy, and avoid the nerve with your strength."
"And if the vein is between two nerves, then you should cut the vein lengthwise."
"The benefit of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is that it draws blood from the head and relieves illnesses of the eyes."
"And therefore in this case, if you want the phlebotomist to puncture with an olive-shaped or myrtle-shaped phlebotomy instrument, because it is the safest of all veins, for it has neither artery nor nerve underneath."
"But nevertheless, sometimes an aposteme occurs in some people when it is not phlebotomized in the first percussion, but do not touch that aposteme."
"The first of these is that you should know that phlebotomy is administered only for the conservation of health and in hope of its benefit and caution against the occurrence of illnesses, and that it should be performed on one of the veins which are in the elbow, namely the cephalic, basilic, or median cubital vein; and that it should be performed at the beginning of spring, after fasting in spring and autumn, and in winter close to meals, when signs of repletion appear."
"And phlebotomy should be performed on the third day or at least one day after three hours have passed since the previous bloodletting."
"But phlebotomy which is administered when it provokes convenience and necessity at that time, whether now or later, on any day or hour or time."
"And children should not be phlebotomized until they are at least fourteen years old, and women who are pregnant should not be phlebotomized except from the fourth to the seventh month, and elderly people who have already passed seventy years should not be phlebotomized."
"And when someone wants to be phlebotomized for any reason, they should cleanse their bowels beforehand with a lion's claw purgative, especially if there is much retained fæces in them, so that the veins do not draw the superfluities into the phlebotomy of the bowels which are purified, and do not harm the principal members."
"And patients with sacred nausea and gluttony should not be phlebotomized until they have removed themselves from their illness or drunkenness, nor those suffering from dropsy, and moreover phlebotomy should be feared near colic pain and vomiting, near abdominal tumors, and near multiplication of coitus and work and exercise during fasting or vigils or for any cause that dissolves virtue, whether corporeal or animal."
"And also consider the subtlety of the humors before that, if the blood is thick, with foods and drinks, and with medicines if it is possible to do so."
"And also take a bath if there is no prohibition for doing so, or stimulate with some exercise so that the blood may be subtilized."
"And pause the phlebotomy of it in the evening, around the hour of Terce, as we have said."
"And perform that evening as guarded in that day from all animal bad accidents, such as anxiety, anger, fear, and from all corporeal accidents, such as work and superfluous effort and coitus and their like."
"And at his seat, let the causes be present in the use of which men are accustomed with aromatics and fragrances and with balms and their like according to the quantity of possibility."
"And follow the phlebotomist on a cushion that is higher than the cushion of the one who is to be phlebotomized."
"And also extend his arm, and rub that with his hand the phlebotomist two or three times; and also stretch the limb with a bandage and wrap it twice."
"And let the bandage be moderate, for a bandage that is not equal can prohibit the flow of blood, or one with superfluity, and likewise one with softening can prohibit the course of the blood equally."
"And also during the bandage, let him who is to be phlebotomized rub his hands together within them until the veins are inflated and declared to the touch."
"And also the phlebotomist should anoint his scalpel with a little ancient olive oil properly; and also let him place his index finger of his left hand over that vein, away from the location where the phlebotomy is desired, so that the vein may yield somewhat, and the percussion be situated where one of the veins which underlie the phlebotomy is struck, like strings fleeing beneath it, and those are full of windiness, and when the scalpel pauses upon them they contract beneath the scalpel, and the phlebotomist descends; and the scalpel does not open the vein, and if it opens, that vein is narrow."
"And therefore, let the phlebotomist be firm and attentive to all these causes."
"And also let him pause the scalpel, but if the vein is opened in one attempt, that is good; and if not, then repeat that elsewhere away from that location somewhat, or above it with haste, if the location is not inflamed."
"And if the location is inflamed or the illness worsens, then let him leave it for a day or two, and do not stretch the limb, for otherwise it would draw an ulcerous inflammation, nor take a bath."
"And also repeat the phlebotomy if necessary."
"And if the opening is small and blood flows subtly, and you fear that it will not let out the desired quantity of blood, then repeat the scalpel in that track according to rectitude and adjust the opening somewhat, and do this with haste before the location becomes inflamed, for many people have inflammation occur due to a few openings."
"But if the location is already inflamed, then do not repeat upon it, for it serves no purpose; and pause upon it some part of the fold of olive oil, for that alleviates the course of blood." "And it is better in this location to use olive oil than any other oils."
"Similarly administer oil during any phlebotomy to prevent veins from prohibiting the flow of blood. They do this with theriac and sagazena when placed upon one of those locations, because blood becomes subtler and revolves when it's thick."
"If a large boil forms at the site of phlebotomy, as often happens, especially if not much blood has been drawn previously, or when the vein is barely opened, then you should, immediately after, place on that location a damp sponge infused with water and salt, slightly warmed, and hold it there for an hour, as this will dissolve it."
"You should also do that near the site of bloodletting from that vein with some vinegar or from another vein."
"But if some blackness or greenness remains at the site for several days, do not worry about it. And if you wish, place over that area some plaster of aloe and myrrh dissolved in vinegar, or some plaster of mint juice or chamomile juice or something similar."
"And many times it happens that boils and swellings occur which need phlebotomy at the basilica."
"And then place your hand over it, and if you suddenly feel softness, apply pressure with your hand on that swelling, for at that moment the artery is flowing strongly. And then protect yourself by placing some of what we have said upon it, because a lot of blood flows from the artery."
"And make an emplaster with what is styptic, so that the location remains firm. And treat it with other treatments until it heals."
"And you should draw blood from each person according to the quantity of their strength and according to the prevailing color that appears above the blood."
"For if the blood is black, let it flow until it becomes red. And similarly, if you find it thick, let it flow until it thins out. And likewise, if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness subsides."
"And for one who is full and strong and needs bloodletting at once, the phlebotomy of a vein should be increased and the phlebotomy wound made wide."
"And in one who is frail, do the opposite of that, and let blood from him little by little over several days, and let the wound be narrow."
"It is better to make a transverse section in phlebotomy of a vein than to make a puncture."
"And this manner of phlebotomy saves from the flow of blood and from injury to nerves. And it is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy made according to width and length."
"And for one who is accustomed to fainting during phlebotomy, you should treat him beforehand with a little bread infused in rue wine or simple celery seed wine and scraped. And let blood from him three or four times."
"And if it is coldness of temperament, then take beforehand in phlebotomy bread infused in myrrh syrup or in aromatic honey syrup with spices such as pepper, cinnamon, ginger and the like, or in syrup that is aromatic and very fragrant."
"And if fainting occurs during phlebotomy and it is due to the loss of much blood, then you should give him to drink water of meat and very fragrant syrup. And use aromatics with almonds, and let him lie down on his left side."
"And use the other causes that we have mentioned in the division in the chapter of fainting due to evacuation."
"And whoever wants to stimulate his arm and dissolve his blood on the second day should let blood from one who has had much evacuation by phlebotomy, and whose virtue is frail. Let it flow little by little, according to the quantity of his strength over several continuous days."
"However, whoever wants to stimulate his arm and draw his blood again on the second day, and if his body is strong, then do this about seven or nine hours after the first phlebotomy."
"However, whoever wants to draw blood from his body contrary to the part that declines, let it be done on the second or third day."
"However, in a body where there is little blood already scraped and it is sharp and causes fever, let blood be drawn from him once and let much blood flow. And make the opening wide so that fainting may occur, for you have preserved all the conditions of phlebotomy. And place your hand over the pulse of his artery before letting blood, so that he does not die instead of fainting, because many times this happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligent."
"And when you want to stimulate the arm and evacuate blood again on the second day, it is not necessary to do so if the opening of the vein has already been closed and it is difficult to draw blood. Then press upon that with force and curve it gently, for that is too bad."
"Let him lie down until he can be phlebotomized again. Or remove that with the acuteness of the flebotomy which is coagulated from the blood in the opening of the vein. Or place upon it tyriaca alfarach or sage, and press upon it gently until the blood flows out."
"And if the vein is inflamed, then leave that alone and do not touch it until the inflammation has subsided."
"And if there is a need to provoke you to evacuate blood again and there is no excuse for it, then do so on that same spot or on the other arm in another vein."
"And the vein called 'the rope of the arm' is phlebotomized in place of the cephalic or the basilica when they are not found or are obscured, for it is composed of those."
"And the quality of phlebotomizing him is that the patient should put his hand in warm water until the vein appears prominently."
"And phlebotomize strongly upon that with a bandage with moderate tightness. And phlebotomize according to the transverse line, not latitudinally nor according to length."
"And let the opening be wide and let your phlebotomy be such that you phlebotomize him on the junction of his hand."
"And if the flow of blood is prohibited, then immerse your hand in warm water again and let the blood run into the water until you achieve your purpose."
"However, if it is summer, then you are excused from immersing your hand in warm water. And in winter, the body of the blood should be kept warm in warm water."
"And phlebotomizing this vein is safer than all other veins because there are neither pulsating veins nor nerves in its vicinity."
"And phlebotomizing the wrist of the right hand corresponds to diseases of the liver."

"And thus ends the phlebotomy."

Of these, one is on the nape of the neck, which is the last part of the head; the middle location between two parts of the collarbone; the chin below the mandible near the mouth; the spatulas' locations; the alocostrum, which is above the end of the ribcage; the forearms, two on each arm; and the cavities, as well as the buttocks."
"And bloodletting does not extract blood from the large veins but rather from the small ones scattered throughout the flesh, and therefore it does not draw out the vital spirits in that way, just as phlebotomy is said to do; nor should bloodletting be performed with fire or without fire in any illness caused bypletion until the whole body has been evacuated."

"And the term of scarification is only as deep as the skin."

"And for one whose excess fluids are thick, wash their vents with old wine, or water of hyssop, which is majoram, or decoction of dill, or chamomile, and their like."
"And it is necessary that the ventosing be done before bathing; having to be administered near to the time of bathing for one hour or two, and having to sleep near to the ventosing."

"And the ventosas of the weak and emaciated are different from those of the young and robust."

"And immediately it should be paused on the member of the sick person, and let him be over the wick that we have said, until when we have the ventosas and do not need to hold the wick and let out the vapor over that wick, then the ventosa will soon dissolve."
"And then put out the meca according to its manner, and repeat it if necessary."

"And this is its form: And this ventosa, the larger it is, the better; for it can receive a large quantity of water."
"Chapter XCVI."
"Sanguissugas are not administered on most members but on those where it is not possible to place ventosas because of their smallness, such as the throat and gums and similar ones; or on a member denuded of flesh, such as the finger and nose and their similars."
"And it is said that sanguissugas should be used when found sanguissugas that are in the muddy waters of ditches."
"And they should be placed during the day and night in muddy water until they are hungry, and that none remain in the belly of them; and immediately the body should be evacuated at the beginning with flebotomy and ventosas; and immediately rub the member of the sick person until it becomes reddened; and immediately pause them on it."
"And when they are full, remove them."
"If suction from the place with ventosa is possible, it should be preferred in judgment."
"But if not, then wash the place with vinegar and immediately with warm water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed."
"And if the body of blood is prolonged immediately after the removal of the sanguissugas, and it remains there, then you put a cloth dipped in hot water on it until the bleeding stops."
"And if the blood is multiplied, then dust it with crushed vitriol, or guallas and similar ones of the causes stipticas until the bleeding stops; or pause on the place many of cooked beans, and let them be left there until the beans adhere to the place and the bleeding stops."
"And it is necessary, if necessary, to repeat the sanguissugas when it is possible to change those."
If bloodletting is prohibited by suspension, then let fresh blood be drawn from the area or place a needle in the spot where blood will come out; and immediately cover it up, for those who feel any blood soon faint.
When you want them to faint, sprinkle a bit of ground aloe over that area, or salt, or ashes, and then cover it up immediately.
Thus begins the third part: On Restoration. This chapter is often necessary in the art of medicine, and concerns the restoration of fractures and dislocations of bones that occur by chance.
Oh, son, know that those who brag about this chapter are foolish doctors and laymen, and those who at any time do not regard the books of the Ancients nor read even one letter from them.
And for this reason it is that this method of the Ancients in our land has been lost; and you will not find anyone now who knows all about it. And I have not added what follows here, except to make reading easier from the books of the Ancients and understanding their meaning better, so as to extract knowledge from them.
And therefore I direct myself towards experimentation and study throughout my life.
And therefore I have already described in this chapter all that my knowledge contains and on which my experience has been passed. Since here I have shortened it for you others and freed myself from the occupation of lengthening it; and have shortened it at the end of shortening; and have shown it at the end of declaration; and have formed many forms of instruments that are administered in that, as is done by addition of declaration, as I have done in two previous chapters.
Chapter One
Summary and Collections from the Essence of Bone Fractures, Before Beginning Remembrance of Members Broken in Various Ways and Their Diversities if Possible. Therefore, I shall now state the summaries of this discourse in the prologue of this chapter; and the chapters that are necessary for understanding at the beginning for artisans and for standing firm on the truth of these [summaries], so that it is you and those who are eager to know or learn this noble art other than you.
And therefore I say that when some fracture or dislocation or torsion or luxation occurs, it is necessary at the beginning to perform phlebotomy on that [person], or to apply a poultice, or whatever seems appropriate, if no prohibition prevents it, such as debility of virtue, or if the person affected is an infant, or elderly, or if it is time of great heat, or cold.
And moreover it should be content with its own food of fresh herbs and of the flesh of birds and of crabs; and prohibit thick meats and filling foods and any food that fills the veins with blood, so that you may be sure of avoiding an abscess, and if not afraid of effusion of matter in the member.
And then return the patient to its first regimen, with which it is accustomed to live.
And therefore when the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary to pasture the patient with nutritious foods of great and heavy nourishment in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and capons, and fish, and intestines of cows, and eggs, and fresh cheese, and thick wine, and similar ones, and with this regimen coagulation of the fracture is effected more easily and quickly.
And know that bones broken when they are in the first youth or old age in men cannot be joined and consolidated according to their nature by drying of the members to which they belong.
But nevertheless nature adjusts and adapts itself to the broken bone in such a way that it adheres to the place where it has been separated, growing thicker there until it is joined together and consolidated, until the end of strength and firmness, as fire at the beginning, unless some cause prohibits it from its works.
And for this reason it is necessary that you pause the foods of the patient with foods in which there is gravity and viscosity and thickness, as we have said.
Fractures differ according to the variety of limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from that of the head; and a fracture of the foot bone is diverse from that of the hand; and similarly all other limbs are diverse amongst themselves.
And you have come with the memory of every kind of fracture, explained in its own chapter, separate from the others.
Many kinds of fractures occur within itself. And the kinds of bone fractures are diverse in themselves; for example, an arch fracture can occur in the stars, and sometimes they are made according to the length of the bone: such as those of the satire and asterix, additional procedures, and non-procedural ones, like when a pen is prepared to write, and it is a fracture with swelling and disruption at the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And in every species there exists its own torment proper to the restoration of that which will come later, remembering it distinctly in its places.
Many signs of bone fracture. "Therefore it is recognized as a fracture of the bone: it is twisted, and from that prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and it becomes so when you grasp it with your hand."
"If there is no apparent twisting and no deformity felt when touching the bone with agitation, and the patient does not feel great pain, then it is not a fracture."
"It is possible that there may be some twisting or small fissure present, so do not move it with your hand and compress it all at once. Instead pause on it and apply the appropriate medicine from which you have a reminder at that moment, as this will be beneficial for the location; especially if it is narrow due to subtle stricture."
"And you should know that when the place is curved and not split in two or otherwise broken during the examination, if it is entirely separated from its comparison, then you must begin immediately with the correction of it so that it may become adherent by cautery."
"And if an adherent cauterization occurs there, then leave it alone for several days until the cautery stops. And again, lift it up and engage it according to each method that you can use."
"And you should know that the restoration of that place and the cauterization of it are easier than on bone where such examination has taken place."
"And also strengthen that according to what comes to your memory of it."
"But if you have examined the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken member in two parts, whether it is the hand or foot; or with your own hand if the member is small; or with two strings or with one string and your hand."
"And you should position yourself in such a way that the member is placed where it was originally located according to its natural shape, while the broken parts are being extended. Then perform the following: bring those additions back with engagement and according to each method that you can use in their locations."
"And beware according to your strength that no harm comes upon the sick through your operation on them or their injury. And perform yourself according to your ability, applying one bone with its companion according to their best forms."
"And it is necessary that you touch that one appropriately with your hand at that time. And if sometimes some different cause arises, correct and engage it according to the quantity of your virtue."
"And avoid violent extension and compression, as some foolish doctors do who often cause adherent cauterization to occur due to their operation or cause contraction in the member, as we have seen happen many times."
"And the quality of the restriction of the broken member is what I recommend. Therefore, know that broken members are diverse in their smallness and greatness and in their forms."
"And of those that are small, such as the arm, hand, and foot, and similar ones, then the bandage should be made of very fine cloth."
"But those that are large, such as the thigh or calf, require a firm and strong bandage. For the bandage tightly connects the large member with equal restriction on all sides so that nothing penetrates into it."
"And then begin gently with your evacuation of the bone, placing a suitable linen there on the location of that fracture with three or four bandages according to what the member deserves. And tighten your hand slightly with the linen."
"And then take that one to the uppermost part of the location of the fracture and tighten more than you had previously tightened at the location of the fracture." "And then extend yourself with a bandage from the location of the fracture somewhat and slowly loosen the restriction little by little until you take hold of it in one great piece."
"And then take another bandage and wrap it around the broken location with a bandage. And then go with those bandages to the part farthest from the fracture, and your operation should be in the restriction of the bandage and in its loosening according to what was said regarding the first bandage above."
"And then pause between the bandages with a piece of lion's skin or cloth with which the torsion of the fracture may be adapted if there is any torsion. And if not, do not add anything in it."
"And then place another bandage over it and adapt it over those bandages that are decently made. If there is no inflammation or adherent cauterization in the location, then pause on what sits beneath the cauterization or removes the inflammation. And leave that for a few days. And then tighten the bandages."
"And let the splints be made of strong cane split lengthwise and skillfully formed; or they may be made of hazel wood; or of palm branches. And let each splint be shaped according to this form and figure:"
"However, the splint placed on that fracture should be thicker and firmer than the other splints, and its length should be proportional to the size of the member. And then tighten over the splints with another bandage according to your first restriction. And then tie it above with a knot made decently as was said regarding restriction. And let your restriction be greater at the location of the fracture, and the further away from the fracture, the less the restriction should be."
"And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and made of soft wool. And if the threads are thick, as I have seen some fools using ropes for binding, it is a great error when the restriction is unequal."
"And very fine threads are not convenient because you will not be able to tighten with them as much as you want. And there should not be less than one finger's breadth between splint and splint."
"If the patient feels pain at the ends of the splints near the restriction in the swollen places, then pause those with lion's skin or woolen cloth until that ceases to cause injury."
"Sometimes fracture occurs with contusion, and dislocation happens in the shoulder joint. I have spoken about that one in the chapter on singularities."
"And know that not every broken member should be immediately bound with splints on the first day. The reason is that when a member is large, it does not require splints to be placed upon it until about five or seven days have passed, according to your certainty of the arrival of an adherent cauterization."
"And this is the manner of splints with which the Ancients used to restore fractures and dislocations and torsions."
"The manner of splinting commonly used for the restoration of fractures, convenient for many complexities and especially for women and children, is this: Take some powder of wheat flour, which is the meal that remains after grinding. Place it in a bag made of fine cloth or leather, and apply it over the place where there is no heat nor friction."
"And comfort him thus: sift that powder with egg white, then pause the mixture's preparation not too thick nor thin; and then use it."
"Another method for making a plaster to reduce fractures and dislocations: take assafetida, lapdanum, acacia, enula, and millet seed, which is the seed of wild celery, all four ounces each; mirra and aloen, each five drachms; alochil, twenty drachms; all are ground and sieved and mixed with almond oil or egg white if the patient's complexion is scalded. Use this plaster, for it has a good composition and helps to heal lightly broken bones."
"Another method for making plaster that helps with fractures and dislocations: take millet seed, white metheglin (honey mead), mirra, aloen, acacia, and gum arabic, each in the amounts specified. Mix them together well."
"All these are ground together in a mortar with wheat flour, then sieved and mixed with water or egg white before administration."
"Method for plastering dislocations and separated bones in their locations, to relieve accidental pain therein and for cracks and fractures: take raw wool and submerge it in sour wine and oil of cypress; then place it on the location."
"It should be noted that this plaster does not have a virtue for restoring fractures or drawing out pus, but rather it serves to relieve pain directly."
"Another method for making plaster that restores broken bones: take fig leaves and poppy leaves, both fresh; mix them together evenly to make the plaster."
"An abbreviated method for plastering to aid in the restoration of bone fractures and when you want resolution of the remnant of pus: take equal parts of rose leaves, chamomile flowers, violet flowers, and barley flour; grind them all together and mix with wine if the member is not scaled."
"But if it is scaled, use that made with wet coriander water or simply water and egg white instead."
"Another method for making plaster that is very strong in resolving abscesses when they occur during the restoration of a member: take equal parts of rose leaves and their seeds, balm, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, and wheat bran; grind them all together and mix with water from sauce plants or sweet water or wine, depending on the quality of the member and the appearance of its color."
"But Hippocrates does not specify in his book that it should be placed on the broken member during its restoration, but rather he recommends a cerate made from wax and oil, not otherwise. And he advises that it should be of medium thickness."
"But it is seen in Galen that they should be placed on the broken member during its restoration, and he lists the causes for which it is used with less heat, such as myrrh, incense, and similar substances, according to those causes that we have recommended."
"Method for making plaster that relieves injury and pain: take myrtle, cypress leaves, human hair shavings or chicken feathers, rose leaves, and salt; mix them all together evenly; grind and sieve them, then mix to make the plaster."
"But the quantity that should remain on the plaster according to how it should be removed and properly loosened is this: if there is no pain or swelling, nor is the bone broken, then do not remove it for several days." "And if severe pain or swelling or inflammation occurs in that place, then you should remove it immediately or cut it away in that hour and remove the plaster from there."
"And take a cloth and a damp sea sponge, and submerge that in hot water. With it, wash the affected area until the swelling subsides and the pain is calmed. Then let the member rest for an hour. Next, place clean wool soaked in vinegar and oil or sweet oil on it, and leave it there overnight to ensure that the abscess is cauterized and that the inflammation of the member has passed, and that the pain has been relieved."
"And then reapply the plaster gently and in a small amount, not binding too tightly with your first bandage, until it is healed."
"And if it happens that the abscess, redness, pain, inflammation, and all other accidents have been entirely relieved, and you still need plaster and bandaging, then apply it again in the same way as you did at the beginning."
"And if the member does not experience any of those causes that we have mentioned, then do not remove the plaster until three or four or five or seven days have passed. And sometimes it is left on for twenty days according to how the healing of the member appears, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and approaches the coagulation of the flesh upon it."
"And then adjust the bandage more tightly than your first one, and adjust it according to the swelling of the wound, as healing progresses."
"But if you find that the site of the fracture has dried out and wasted more than it should, then know that nutrition is prohibited from reaching it. Therefore, moisten it with hot water every time you remove it; do this for all three days. And loosen the bandage a little, and by this operation, you will bring nourishment to the member, and it will heal easily."
"But those who foolishly restore a fracture of the member at another time when it has not been set properly from the beginning, and is set incorrectly with crookedness, is an error according to their operation, and causes great fear. And if this were done correctly, the Ancients wrote about it in their books and performed it thus."
"And I have not seen any of them do that, so it is more reasonable that they should not do it."
"Chapter Two: On fracture when it occurs in the head."
"The types of fractures are many, and their shapes are diverse. And the causes are different, because some fractures are caused by impact from a blow, which leaves all the bone inside the skull, leaving the bone bare like wood, and this type is called "exulsion." Or the blow cuts off some part of the bone and cuts the flesh around it without penetrating to the inner side of it. This type of fracture is called "avulsion absolute."
"The severity of these two fractures is either great or slight, and their cause is percussion with heavy or sharp force."
"This fracture may be penetrating, approaching the panniculus which is attached to the bone, or it may be on the surface of the bone. The severity of these two fractures is either great or slight."
"This fracture is one that is confined within the bone, in its finest capillary subtlety, and for this reason it is called a capillary fracture."
"This fracture occurs from a blow or percussion with heavy force, causing some of the bone's laminae to enter into its interior, similar to what happens to egg shells when they are struck and their insides crack."
"Especially this occurs in the bodies of moist bones, such as those of children's heads."
"And thus all these species of fractures are painful, like separated stars." "I have come to remind you of all this in its proper place."
"And I know all these species of fracture by their detection through the skin and by inquiry with touch and inspection of rotting flesh that is over them."
"The capillary species is recognized by this: it appears on the bone and is wiped clean; then it is touched with the hand, as the fracture looks black."
"But in the treatment of fractures, first consider the accidents of the illness. If there are any accidents that indicate the presence of fear by manifest signs, such as vomiting from cholera, extension and loss of consciousness, sudden loss of voice, syncope, acute fever, dilation of pupils, redness of eyes, and similar accidents in major conditions, then it is not healed."
"But if there are no current accidents, then you may expect recovery, and at that point begin the treatment of it."
"The reason for this is that if it happens to you at the beginning as it happened, and especially during the winter days, then you should study the appearance of the bone before the 14th day according to every condition."
"And if it happens in the summer days, then you should uncover the bone before the seventh day, having ensured that what remains of the bone has been corrupted by the flesh, and that the accidents we have mentioned occur."
"And if the fracture has already reached the flesh covering the brain and is in many small parts and adherent, then you should insert the comminuted and adherent fragments according to what I will tell you."
"And so you should shave the head of the patient and uncover the bone according to your usual custom and manner as best you can, following the shape of the fracture, ensuring that it is light on the patient."
"And if it happens to you that the detection of the bone is difficult, with fluxes of blood or hard abscess, then you should postpone the operation with whatever is necessary, and this is what you should do: apply dressings infused in wine and rose oil to the place, and let the wound rest until the abscess has subsided and you are sure that there is no bleeding; then begin with the circumcision of the bone and the ablation of it."
"And this is according to one of two ways of operating."
"And the first way is that you insert the bone with an incision of such a size using an incision tool; and this is its form:
"And next administer a larger dressing than the second one."
"And you should have various dressings available, some of which are larger than others; some are shorter than others and have sharper edges, made of iron or steel."
"And use them with ease in percussion on the dressing so that you do not strike the head and hurt the patient."
"And if the bone is strong and hard, you should first perforate around it before administering dressings with non-penetrating tweezers."
"And these are not called thus because those do not reach the term of the bone's end where it is appropriate, so that the tweezers have a rounded extremity or margin over that which is left behind the head sharp like a margin or small circle, which should be prevented from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone."
"And it is convenient for you to take many of these tweezers which fit the thickness of each bone, so that there are enough present in all tests of the brain whose extremities are sharp, and their quantity in length and width should be according to the quantity of thickness of this."
"And this is the shape of three kinds of tweezers:
"Impede the fracture with an incision using scissors, and gently squeeze out as much pus as possible from between each broken bone towards the end of the elevation, as mentioned earlier, until you can radiate or remove the bone with your hand or any other tool suited for this purpose, such as tweezers."
"And make sure that the scissors do not touch any part of the patient's body."
"And then, once you have circled the bone perforation and separated it from the patient if it is continuous with them, you will extract it using a tool similar to scissors but more jagged and sharp than others."
"And if there are any broken small bones and you try to put them back together easily using whatever tools are available, then dress the wound with linen and bandages as mentioned earlier."
"And another way of operating is gentle and far from frightening, which Galen describes and praises highly. This is the method: it is necessary for you to begin by uncovering the bone fracture site as soon as possible and clearly."
"And the lenticular cut is made without cutting anything else, and the acute cut is made on both sides along its length like this: so that the lenticular cut fits tightly against the patient with the sharp face of the bone facing inwards."
"And then strike the scissors of one cut with a small hammer until you have easily cut through all the bone in this way. And be sure that no accident happens to the patient, and if the doctor was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant, certainly he would be asleep."
"Then if there are any attachments of the soft tissue to the bone in certain places, cut those with the lenticular end of the scissors and remove them easily, for they are removed without harm or fear."
"And if the bone fracture does not penetrate into the soft tissue, and what is cut off or scraped from the bone is its surface, which remains rough and with delicate projections, then you must shave off that roughness and remove those projections using fine razors which you have prepared in different quantities so that you may use them conveniently and correctly according to how you plan your operation on that broken bone and its shape."
"And you must use in your rasping of the bone at the beginning the widest razors, and then immediately use the most subtle ones, and continue doing this until you arrive at using the thinnest and finest of all."
"But in each of the other small capillary fractures and subtle fractures, you must provide care according to what you have planned for their correction."
"And this is not mentioned among all those who have studied that art." "And concerning what has been said, we have shown it in large fractures."
"And if the soft tissue is torn when you are removing the bone, then you must take cloth according to the amount of the wound and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another piece of cloth folded or tripled, immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it gently on top of the first cloth without pressing down too hard. Then apply a wide bandage, and do not tie it too tightly but only according to the amount that the wound requires."
"And leave it with this dressing for one or two days until you are sure that there is no suppuration. Then remove the dressing and apply medicines that promote dryness, such as linseed resin and iris flour, powder of incense and of aloe, and similar ones."
"And if you make these medicines into powders that are sprinkled on the wound, they should be dry. In general, apply any medicine that has the property of cleansing and not biting."
"And make sure that the wound in your care is clean and not foul with grease or anything else. Do not let it become infected with virulent matter, for if it does, it will corrupt the soft tissue of the brain and cause illness."
"And if inflammation occurs in the head wound, especially after the bone has been scraped and when care is not taken, it will appear black on its surface. Then consider that you have caused the harmful accidents mentioned earlier, and know that they are fatal without doubt."
"If there is no blackness but only from the medicine applied on top, then it is the virtue of that medicine which causes the blackness. Therefore, take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them together with a cloth, and apply it to the inflammation. Then treat it according to the usual methods of care until it heals."
"Chapter 3"
"On the restoration of the nose when it is crushed."
"You should know that the nose is not broken except by two parts being crushed together and pushed upwards, or one of those parts being displaced. This happens because there are two bones in the nose, the left one being made of cartilage and not broken, but rather becoming bruised and distorted."
If one of the patient's limbs is broken, then you should insert the little finger into its socket and immobilize that fracture using your thumb and index finger from within, and your palm externally, until it returns to its natural shape. And do this carefully, ensuring you don't hurt the patient during this procedure.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nostril and your finger cannot reach it, then use the tip of a small stick instead.
If the fracture involves both parts, proceed as follows:
Try to restore it on the first day of the fracture if possible; otherwise, wait seven or nine days for the swelling to subside before doing so.
And insert a cloth wick into the nostril tip if the fracture involves one part, or two cotton pads if it involves both parts, ensuring that they are thick enough to fill the nasal passage.
Some ancient surgeons claim that cotton pads should be bandaged at the edges and changed daily, but I do not find this necessary. Instead, wrap the wick in a thin layer of album made from egg white mixed with powdered myrrh; leave it in place until the bone is healed and the cartilage has regained its strength.
Sometimes, instead of cotton pads, a linen cloth folded into the shape of a cone may be inserted into the nostril to prevent any accumulation of pus within it. This can be done even if the nasal fracture is severe and the patient has difficulty breathing through their nose.
You don't need to do this; if you wish, you may use the linen cloth instead of cotton pads, but it is not necessary.
And if another abscess forms later on, apply a plaster made of wax or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or diacylon.
But if an abscess does not form, you should then plaster the outside of it with ground sesame seeds and powdered myrrh mixed with egg white. Place a linen strip over this plaster, and do not manipulate the nose in any way.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are merely comminuted, you should then cut over them and extract those fragments using appropriate instruments. Then suture the wound and treat it with substances that promote healing and consolidation, along with suitable plasters.
And if a deformity occurs inside the nose, then you should treat it using cotton pads and lead cannulae until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV
On the restoration of the jawbone when it is broken.
When the jawbone is broken and the fracture does not heal properly, examine whether the fracture is displaced only on one side and not completely shattered or bent inward.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth and similarly for the left mandible. Then gently push the fracture inward with this finger while supporting it from outside with your other hand.
If the jawbone fracture is already separated into two parts, then you must apply traction to both parts in a straight line until it is possible to reduce the fracture.
If teeth are displaced or loosened due to the fracture, then strengthen those that you expect may break with gold, silver, or silk thread. Apply pressure gently on the broken jawbone using cotton pads and then place a folded towel, a piece of gauze, or a sandbag along the length of the jawbone to maintain its position until reduction is achieved without causing further damage.
Tell the patient to rest and avoid activities that require excessive jaw movement for a while, especially eating hard or chewy foods.
If you notice any changes in the patient's appearance at any time after reduction and fixation of the fracture, remove the splint on the third day. Then examine the area for any signs of infection or other complications, remove any remaining cotton, and apply a dressing using powdered borax, egg albumen, or flour to promote healing, followed by a bandage.
Once the dressing and splint are applied correctly without altering the position of the bone, leave them in place until healing is complete and the fracture is stable. Often, these fractures heal within three weeks.
If an abscess forms later on, follow these steps until it heals and is drained:
"If the fracture is in a visible place, then consider if it's a separation of bone fragments or shattered pieces. Then subtly remove those shattered pieces with whatever tools are appropriate for their removal."
"If the opening of the wound is narrow, then widen it using the forceps according to your need."
"And when you have removed those shattered pieces and there is no more left to remove, then if the opening of the wound is wide enough, proceed with your treatment. But if not, apply one of the plasters that are appropriate for this purpose and cover it until it heals."
"Chapter Five."
"On the restoration of the clavicle when it is fractured."
"The clavicle is often broken at its middle third, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways:"
"Either the clavicle is broken into two separate pieces with no shattered fragments, which is easy to restore; or it is separated in such a way that there are shattered fragments, which makes restoration difficult; or the fracture occurs with an open wound."
"And the operation in this case, when the fracture is closed, is that you have two assistants present, one to hold the retractor and follow the broken clavicle, and the other to extend the patient's neck at that location."
"And once you have exposed the fracture with the said tongs, make sure that the bone ends fit together properly according to their original shape, without any prominence or concavity."
"And if you need more extension, then you should place a wad of cloth or wool behind the patient's shoulder to support it, according to how much is needed. Then extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the wad with your hand until the fracture is properly reduced."
"And if you cannot bring the end of the clavicle back into its proper position because it has become shortened, then have the patient sit up on their shoulders and place a pillow or cushion under their upper chest."
"And the assistant presses on the muscle at that location while you elevate the clavicle until it is in its proper position. Then you can reduce the fracture and secure it with the said tongs."
"But if there are any sharp edges or spikes on the clavicle, you should cut them off and remove them carefully."
"And if the clavicle is retained in the bone, then you should make an incision through it using one of the scalpel blades that you have prepared for this purpose. You had previously placed a retractor on the clavicle to expose the instruments, which are made of wood or iron and have this shape:"
"It is shaped like a spatula with this concavity. Its width should be according to your needs, depending on the size of the bone or its thinness."
"But its length should be as short as possible for the operation, with two ends like this: one end is wide and the other is narrow."
"But if the disruption of the wound that you made when removing the spike from the bone is large and likely to form a hematoma, then close the wound edges with sutures."
"But if the disruption is small or you fear a hematoma, then fill the wound with dressings and powders according to the size of the wound."
"And if a hematoma forms, then soak a cloth in sour wine and rose oil and apply it over that area."
"And all of this is done while avoiding putting pressure on the fractured bone. And you should see the patient every day to ensure that the bandage does not become loose or slip off as the swelling decreases. If it does, then correct it and tighten the bandage."
"And also make the patient sleep on their back with a small pillow placed at their side at night. This will elevate the adjutors of their shoulder and thus elevate the fracture of the clavicle and reduce strain on the muscles, or keep the arm bound to their chest."
"And do not remove the bandage until about twelve days have passed unless there is persistent redness or an abscess in that location."
"And also renew the plaster if you see that its face is dry by then, and adjust it to fit the swelling, restore it, and leave it in place until the fracture of the clavicle has healed and united."
"And healing is usually achieved within twenty-eight days, although sometimes it may take less time in certain individuals."
"Chapter VI"
"On the restoration of fractures of the shoulder."
"A fracture of the scapula usually occurs on its lateral border and does not affect the body of the bone but only the margins."
"When the fracture is located on the medial border of the scapula and not visible except by touch, then prepare the reduction according to its shape and reduce it naturally in every way that you are able."
"And then apply plaster made of fine powdered millstone with egg albumen and linen cloth. Then apply powder made of folded cloth. Then apply a thin bandage around the entire scapula or somewhat wider."
"But if the bandage is concave at some point on the scapula, then fill in that concavity with linen cloth and smooth out the bandage evenly."
"And also secure it above with a long wad of cotton that is gently compressed and ensure that the bandage does not slip from its proper location."
"Check the bandage every day. If it becomes loose at any time, replace it and tighten it again to ensure that the bandage does not slip from its proper location."
"And make the patient lie on their side."
"The scapula is usually restored in twenty days or less."
