"And when this number of days is completed, then remove the bandage, and be sure that you know how to distinguish which bones have solid union without removal."
"And if you are treating a fracture of the humerus and placing it at the side of the elbow, then split it open there and follow what was said regarding the reduction of a hematoma in the clavicle fracture if one occurs here."
"Chapter VII"
"Regarding the healing of a broken foot."
"The foot sometimes breaks in the middle and this can be difficult to heal; but its ends usually grow back together, although they are shortened and fragmented."
"And when the middle of the foot breaks, it declines towards its parts inside and accidents can happen to it with great pain, difficulty in healing, coughing, fever, vomiting blood; and there is an obvious deformity in the cavity of the broken bone which cannot be hidden from touch."
"And for its healing, the patient should be placed on their back, and a pillow should be placed between the soles of their feet; gently press on the muscles of the foot, adjust the bones with two hands, carefully operate and equalize according to any possible manner that is appropriate and convenient, until the figure of the bone returns to its proper position."
"And gently apply plaster and gauze, then place on a thin table board of sauce or chalk and its likes in levitation, since you wrap that up with cloth; carefully apply it onto the broken bone so as not to move it; sometimes tie it around the back according to the roundness, and tighten it with appropriate strapping; regularly check the bandage, and when softened, tighten it again."
"And if there is a need for removal of the bandage due to corruption or inflammation, pain, or an abscess occurring in that place, you should remove and untie it immediately, remove the plaster, correct what has happened with the manner of curing it; and apply plaster again if necessary, sometimes changing its face; adjust the strapping until it is healed."
Capítulo VIII
Chapter VIII
Know that fractures of the ribs occur only in large spaces that are on the back, and at the ends of those bones near their joints it happens only rarely because they are cartilaginous. And knowledge of him is not concealed from His perception despite His inquiry with them.
And the restoration should be such that you align the fracture according to your knowledge with the possible manner, so that it can heal according to need. Afterwards apply plaster and strengthen the broken bone with steel if necessary.
And if the rib fracture extends to its inner partitions, then severe pain and piercing are experienced by the patient, similar to that which is felt by someone who has cried. This happens because the bone pierces the pleura. Difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood also occurs, making it difficult to treat.
And even the Ancients were skilled in treating that with many techniques.
And they said this: it is necessary to withhold food from the patient who has caused inflammation and flatulence, so that the abdomen becomes inflated and extended, pushing the fracture towards its outer partitions.
But we should not do this because it could easily cause an internal abscess if not done carefully. And if one has already occurred, then it can help with that too.
And some of them said that one should suspend wind and then suck it with force; this is similar to reasoning. However, it is feared that the wind might drive the excess fluids to the site due to weakness.
And some of them said: the location should be covered with wool that has been immersed in warm oil, and fine powders should be inserted between the ribs until it is filled. Then, wrap it according to its roundness like a bandage. And treat the patient with pleurisy remedies and medicine.
"If the patient's severe constriction cannot be endured and the bone pierces the membrane with a sharp point, then fear for the patient; therefore we should cut upon that place, break open the shattered rib, and immediately place over that instrument which preserves the circle of remembrance, previously mentioned, and insert the bone gently, extracting that."
"If we need to adjust both lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and heal it with plasters until it is healed."
"If an abscess occurs inside the wound, then cover it and press powder made from rose oil onto the location, until the wound is healed as necessary for an internal abscess."
"And let him be propped up on pillows while he lies upon the bed until he is healed."
Chapter ix.
"Concerning the restoration of fractures of the spine of the back and neck."
Some fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and they sometimes become inflamed, likewise those of the spine. And if it happens to someone and you want to know whether they will be cured or not, then consider this: if sometimes their muscles are already softened and stunned and dead, so that those cannot move nor extend nor shrink those anymore, and when you press those with your fingers or prick those with a needle and do not feel pain in those, then you shall know that it is not healed, and such conditions are mortal.
And if those can move and feel constraint with your fingers and prick there, then you shall know that the neck of the bone is saved and that the illness will recover with treatment.
And if it seems similar in the vertebrae of the neck and you want to know whether they will be healed or not, then consider their weight.
Then if you find that they are already softened like those above, then it has happened to these what has happened to the muscles, and especially when lying on his back, he feels vertigo and evacuation without his will, and sometimes he is suddenly overturned on his back and wants to urinate but cannot. Then you shall know that it is mortal, and at that point do not bother with its treatment.
But if none of those things happen to him, the cause is better and easier to treat.
And the treatment of it is that you pierce into the sedative place of the hard abscess which lies over the injured spine on the right side, or with the mouth of a pouch apply heat there: lie over that several times a day until it is softened the hard abscess.
And then apply one of the comforting and dispersing plasters there, and strengthen it with a bandage. And command to the patient that he use rest and tranquility and do not sleep on the part where he feels pain until it is healed.
"If an accident occurs due to a bone fracture or any other cause that has separated it, then you must cut above the elbow and remove that bone. Then immediately stitch the two edges of the wound with sutures if it is large; then treat it with some ointment from herbs until it heals."
"If the tip of the bone is broken and it's the end of the joint, then you must insert the index finger of your left hand into the anus and gently press on the broken bone with your other hand as much as possible according to the appropriate position; then apply a plaster or bandage if necessary, and then straighten that."
"If you feel something broken inside, then go over there, remove it, and treat the wound with appropriate care until it heals."
Chapter .x
Of the Restoration of Health
"The bones of the arms are sometimes broken and when they break, the fracture is usually located at the ends of those bones and extends along their length towards the interior. In addition to pain in that location and puncture wounds, numbness occurs if it's a fracture."
"And the restoration of this is done by extending your hand over that area until it covers the fracture as shown in the figure."
"And if the fracture is only at the end of it, then you should gently press on that area until it aligns with its natural shape according to your judgment."
"But if the fracture is in the middle or has already shifted towards the interior, then place the patient on their back to prepare for the restoration of that fracture." "And once you've aligned it, apply a plaster and also apply traction using a splint made of wood or metal, being careful not to cause puncture wounds or displacement of the fracture."
"And align the concave surface and press it until it takes on traction according to equality. Then have the patient sleep on their back or side."
"But if an abscess forms later, then stop the extension and restoration until the abscess is drained. Then apply what is needed to drain that according to what has been said earlier; and return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it as necessary."
"And if this happens in the leg bones or they are crushed, then remove them."
"Chapter eleven."
"Regarding the treatment of a broken bone."
"The collarbone, which is between the chest and the head of the shoulder."
"And if it is broken, its treatment can be approached in one of two ways. The first way is to take a piece of wood..."
"and attach two bandages at both ends; it should then be suspended from an elevated place."
"The patient should sit on a chair, then place their broken arm over the board so that its tip reaches the middle point where the curvature of the board is attached."
"Then suspend something heavy from that [point] or the nurse can gently pull it towards the sides. And then the physician will align the fracture with his hands until the fracture returns to its original position."
"The other way is for the patient to lie on their back and suspend their arm with a bandage. Then command two nurses: one should hold what is above the fracture with both hands, and the other should hold the part below it. Each of them should gently pull at their respective ends."
"And if you want to increase the traction force, then place another bandage over the fracture and another one above it. Then each of the two nurses should pull at their respective ends."
"If the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then place half of the bandage on the tip and the other half below the fracture near the elbow."
"And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then place the bandage over that location and also over the elbow."
"Once it is properly aligned and secured without causing distress, then wait for it to be set according to what is necessary and applied well. Then gently stretch it so that there is no heat buildup."
"But if there is heat buildup, then leave the splint on for seven days. Then remove it and apply a clean cloth soaked in warm water and rose oil over where the heat buildup occurred until it subsides. Then gently stretch it."
"The way to gently stretch it is to apply a plaster cast over the fracture. Then place a layer of linen cloth soaked in new wine and oil over the plaster cast. Then adjust the arm using that support and place their hand, covered with a cloth, on top of their muscle."
"And then tie the cloth and gently stretch it over the support for the arm so that the arm is in a straight position, unless prohibited by any reason related to the broken bone."
"And then apply splints."
"And place on the fracture that splint which is widest and strongest among the others. Place it between each finger width apart, and let its length be according to the break with an additional three fingers' width on either side."
"And then apply traction using the splint with the bandage mentioned at the beginning of this chapter."
"And ensure that your traction is applied as closely to the fracture site as possible." "And as the fracture heals, gradually decrease the traction."
"Therefore, if you wish to remove the splints and traction as described in the time when restoring the limb to its original position, then do so."
"And if you fear heat buildup, leave the traction and splints on for seven days, as mentioned earlier."
"Also consider the limb in all cases for three days so that no swelling or inflammation occurs there due to excess pressure from the splint. If necessary, reduce or adjust the diet as prescribed by your physician."
"But if you feel confident that nothing will happen from this, then do not remove the bandage for three days or more."
"And let their sleep be on their back with their arms over their stomach."
"And place a full pillow of wool at the elbow and constantly monitor day and night that the figure of the broken limb does not dislocate or become softened. If it does, then adjust accordingly with your skill."
"And feed the patient according to the order given earlier, ensuring that their food is soft at first until they begin to heal. Then gradually increase the thickness of their food."
"The custom is that the splint and its cloth are strengthened for forty days. Then they should be removed, a bath administered, and the area treated with ointments that will correct it."
"And if the fracture is healed completely, then do not remove the splint and splints for one day or two months."
"Chapter XII."
"On the restoration of broken arms."
"The arm is composed of two bones called 'two forks.' One is small and is located near the thumb, while the other is large and extends from the little finger's side."
"Either the larger fork bone alone can break or both bones together."
"And when the small fork bone is broken at its upper end, then its restoration and healing will be easier."
"But when it is broken near its lower end, the fracture is bad and its healing difficult."
"And worse still is when both bones are broken together."
"Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the small fork bone at its upper end, then it is necessary for the physician to restore it with ease by using a small extension until it is healed."
"But if it is the large fork bone that is broken, then a stronger extension must be used."
"And if both bones are broken together, then traction must be applied very strongly."
"And the figure of the hand must be restored and its extension laid out on a pillow with the palm upwards, placing the thumb highest among all the fingers, the little finger lowest, and the patient centered on a cushioned chair."
"And place a pillow in front of him at his height or slightly below it, so that he does not have to struggle."
"Then one assistant should extend the arm from its lower part with their hand or a bandage, while another extends it upwards in the same manner."
"And then the physician should heal the bone until they have reduced it to the best figure possible."
"If there is any displacement in the fracture of the bone, then forcefully reduce it back into its proper place with your skill."
"If a separated fragment appears that prevents the elbow from extending and you have no hope of restoring it, then perform the procedure described earlier to fix it."
"But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already dealt with that in Chapter X. Therefore, take care of it accordingly."
"And if heat buildup occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then lay a cloth soaked in cerote made from rose oil and white wax over it. The cloth should be neither too thick nor too thin. Then apply gentle pressure with another cloth until the heat subsides. Remove the cerote and apply an emplaster prepared from powdered mallow with clay of eggs and honey. Then apply splints; the splint that is placed over the fracture should be broad and strong."
"You should know that the number of splints for the arm varies depending on the situation, whether it is a fracture in one bone or both bones together."
"Then apply pressure over the site of the fracture very strongly and forcefully. If the extension is directed towards the upper part of the fracture or its lower part, then apply gentle pressure afterwards according to what was mentioned at the beginning of this chapter."
"Let the cloths wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not hard or rough. Let the thread with which it is sewn be neither too fine nor too thick, as we have recommended."
"And consider the limb and its condition after several days." "And if any cause arises requiring correction, such as swelling of the limb, then you should apply cold water compresses until that swelling subsides."
"And let the limb rest without constraint until it regains its usual position."
"And once the inflammation has subsided and the bone is aligned properly, then adjust everything with your skill."
"And also consider if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive constriction; then you should gently relax it slightly and leave it for a few days until the nourishment flows freely again."
"But if no such cause arises as those we have mentioned, then it should not be released within about twenty days or so."
"And then let the patient's arm be suspended by its usual means of movement for a few days, and have him lie on his back to sleep."
"And know that this fracture is usually restored in thirty days or sometimes even thirty-two days, but it could also be restored in twenty-eight days, depending on the individual's constitution and the power of their nature."
Chapter XIII
On the restoration of fractures of the fingers and toes.
"Know that in the case of fractures of the fingers or toes, they rarely happen without some injury to them."
"And when such fracture or injury happens to the hand, then it is necessary for the patient to lie on his back with his head elevated according to equality, and gently suspend the injured arm above him while extended."
"And then the attendant should extend the broken bones and the physician should bind them together so that they are well aligned with good approximation."
"And then it is necessary to apply a plaster and cotton, unless there is an abscess that requires cauterization. Then also apply a splint above according to the size of the location, with that already wrapped in linen."
"But if the fracture is in the middle of the palm near the belly of the hand, then make a similar splint of linen and command the patient to hold it with his palm clasped. Then also secure it with a long strip of linen."
"And let the splint be made of leather covered with linen and elevated so that the leather is applied to the palm. Then secure it according to what is necessary."
"But if the fracture is at the outside of the hand, then it is necessary to apply a splint above and another splint from the little finger side in the palm so that the hand is elevated and straight. Then also apply the bandage as when wrapping around the wrist."
"And if there is a fracture of one of the toes of the foot, then it should be aligned as necessary and then secured with the sole of the foot. And if you wish, apply a small splint on the outside to elevate the fracture and prevent movement."
"But if the fracture is in other toes, such as in the middle toe or the index toe, the ring toe, or the little toe, then it should be aligned and tied with the string of the corresponding toe; or all the toes may be tied together continuously, which is better; or a small splint may be applied to it, as we have said for the big toe."
"And consider, in the restoration of these fractures, and particularly that one, the condition of any abscess formation or opposition that may occur during your operation. Act accordingly when any such cause arises, as described above."
"Chapter Fourteen."
"Of the Reduction of Fractures of the Tibia."
The hip bone of the thigh often breaks and is declared apparent in sensation as it turns to the front and upwards.
And its restoration is done in such a way that the ligament strengthens over the fracture and another ligament leaves the fracture, and the patient is prone on their stomach and face.
And moreover, the surgeon stretches all the ligaments to their destination according to their nature when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it extends to the thigh's ridge, then the ligament attached to the tendon should be pulled towards the front of the knee, so that the tension takes place there, and the other ligament leaves the fracture.
And likewise if the fracture is close to the knee, then it should be done with the ligament attached to the tendon, and its tension should be applied from below.
And moreover the doctor brings the bone together with both of its ends until it reduces according to its natural form and the bone is joined by good union; then it is necessary to apply plaster and compression, otherwise gangrene may occur in the member. But if gangrene occurs, then you should leave it for some days until the gangrene has dried up and then return to your treatment.
The compression should be done thus: first, wrap a hard bandage over the fracture and fold it two or three times, removing any excess; then wrap the limb so that it reaches the ankle and thigh ridge.
And insert a long splint between the thigh and the bandage near the knee below, bringing the ends of the splint from above on both sides, then wrap it around the bandage and thigh with what remains of the bandage.
And then place a pad over the thigh at that fracture point, and place one stake on the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft rags to keep the compression in place.
Then begin with strong compression from the middle over the fracture point with three or four layers, and as you move away from the fracture site, gradually reduce the compression, making it less strong and more flexible.
Then extend both ends of the splint below to reach the ankle bone, and tighten both ends of the other stake on that side to ensure that the ligament does not slip from its position.
Then leave the compression in place with this caution: ensure that there is no injury to the limb, nor gangrene or blisters, nor inflammation or swelling similar to these; and if any of these occur, then carefully remove it and correct whatever has happened immediately, as we have said many times before.
But if the bone sticks out where it was broken, then you should smooth it off as much as possible; and if not, file it down and remove it, and treat with whatever is necessary for healing until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thighbone may become tighter than the stakes used for it, as we have said in the case of the elbow and arm.
But nevertheless in the restoration of the limb in this way there should not occur a limp or limping gait. And if it is restored alone without applying the other thighbone, then that leg will always limp.
"Know that the illness can last for 40 days or slightly more or less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions."
"Chapter XV."
"On the reduction of a tooth fracture."
"You should know that fractures of the tooth crown rarely occur, but they can happen due to strong blows or trauma."
"However, if a fracture does occur, it is usually just a crack or break in its parts, which amounts to very little or somewhat more, and all this is done with caution."
"And the restoration of it is easy, so that what is broken of its parts may be joined together with the said glue and congealed according to what is possible for equality, facility, and manufacture."
"And also apply a plaster and bandage over that round tooth if necessary, and strengthen it with a suitable strapping."
"And also consider all the dispositions which we have recommended for other fractures, such as pus cautery and similar things, so that everything may be applied according to whatever is convenient for it, so that it may heal."
"Chapter XVI."
"On the restoration of a broken arch (crown)."
"Know this: in the hip joint are two bones, one large and named for its resemblance to a chamber, the other subtle and called the trochanter."
"And concerning the various types of fractures that can occur to the two bones of the arm, restoration is made just as it is with dislocation of the arm, and its operation is one."
"And if two bones are crushed at once, the joint becomes completely separated."
"And if only the thinner bone is broken, the joint remains somewhat displaced."
"And if the larger bone is broken alone, it causes immediate paralysis, which is one of the causes that are not amendable."
"And appropriate extension and equination and ligamentation of the fragments should be applied according to that dislocation."
"But nevertheless, if a fracture of the joint is displaced having many fragments, care should be taken to make the extension gentle and lighter, and aided by your virtue."
"And in case of operation on the joint of the arm, this is when you dislocate the fragments and complete all your operation, then you will take pieces of wood from pine that are prepared beforehand for those causes which are placed below, and they should be made equally of branches of palm; choose those that are slender, not thick ones, and their length according to the length of the joint, and place them from the front to behind."
"And appropriately wrap around each splint in two layers according to its length, and place one on the fracture of the joint on one side and the other on the other side, and they should be placed from front to back on the thigh of the leg."
"And appropriately fasten the two splints in three places on both extremities and in the middle, for with this arrangement the joint is prevented from bending to the right or left, and it remains in good position."
"And now a splint of wood according to the length of the joint has been applied; and the joint should be placed upon it so that it retains some movement."
"But particularly when you do this in fractures, especially when there is proper alignment."
"And appropriately examine the joint every day and study it carefully to ensure that you guard against inflammation or infection or other similar causes. And if any of these occurs, then you should apply what is necessary in order for it to be healed."
"Chapter XVII."
"Of the fracture of the ankle and bones of the foot."
"No ankle fracture happened at all; sometimes fractures happen on the foot bones; few foot toes have fractures happening, and they don't cause any trouble in their position."
"And if the foot bones have fractures and those bones are sometimes elevated above each other, then the patient places their foot on the ground prepared in such a way as to walk."
"And he presses one foot onto what is elevated by those bones, and presses on them, squeezing and twisting around the joints."
"And then they apply plaster and bandages on top of it, and place a small board with two flat heads on the front of the foot."
"And then they strengthen that bandage with downward traction, because you have wrapped it around with cloth and other causes as needed."
"And when three or four days have passed, then you untie the bandage, because you will find the bones twisted together, either crushed or displaced, and the operation on them is performed in this way:
"If some teeth are clenched, then restore those, align them thus with glue and stitch them back into place as I have shown you in the restoration of these hands." "And then apply pressure on the bitten finger according to its length, and place it slightly lower than this table that I have shown you earlier, and stretch it with moderate tension."
"But if what is bitten of the bones are two or three or more, then you apply pressure on each finger according to its quantity wrapped in linen cloth, stretch the table towards the bottom of the foot, and stand it upright so that one of its ends rests on the belly of the foot for better retention."
"And remember not to forget that you should not consider all the causes mentioned to you in other fractures of accidents, and apply what is necessary according to each accident."
Chapter xviii
On the fracture of the hip bone of a woman and the pubic bone and penis bone of a man.
When the hip bone of a woman is fractured, then make that cease in this way: wrap it in a bandage, incline it at the junction of both sides slightly, and hold it behind.
And moreover the midwife presses the hip bone from behind, and it is done on the hip bone in this way as I have said earlier; and also the woman is tilted backwards, and raises her buttocks little by little, for all this is forgotten until it is before the junction of the hip bones as I have said earlier and the fractured bone returns.
And moreover powder is applied on the buttocks, and when she wants to urinate, she moves her hip with ease and then urinates, and reduces and returns it according to the manner in which she placed it at the beginning, and again applies the first powder.
And do this for seven days or so until that is restored.
And if you wish, take a bladder of a sheep and stretch it over the fracture of the hip with a reed pipe; and insert all the bladder into the hip bone, and blow through the reed pipe with force until the bladder is inflated within the hip bone, for the fracture returns.
And moreover it should be wrapped in cotton and kept for some days according to what we have recommended until it is healed.
And when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, then for its restoration and splinting, do what we have recommended for the hip bone.
And what is true is not hidden in these fractures near the groin which cause little inconvenience, especially to that person who has some study and use and understands this my book; for that fracture indicates to you in the greatest positions how truly it can be restored and reduced, and therefore understand well.
But in the case of a fractured penis of a man, take a guarguamela (a type of bandage) made of wool, and put it around the penis; and then apply over it a wrapping of cloth, and let it be wrapped up, and then let it lie for three days or so until it is healed.
Chapter XIX
On the restoration of fractures of bones when they are out of place.
"It is suitable for one who has suffered a fracture with profuse bleeding, especially if the bone is large, such as those in the thigh or arm. Quickly perform phlebotomy if the conditions are favorable."
"If the bleeding is profuse and red, then you must use retention to prevent it from flowing further. If there are other causes, treat those as well."
"Therefore begin restoring the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this if no inflammation occurs."
"But if inflammation occurs, then delay the reduction until the seventh day, allowing the inflammation to subside. Do not approach it on the third or fourth day, as you will cause harm."
"But if the bone sticks out above the skin, then you should perform reduction with ease and minimal traction."
"But if you are not prepared for traction and cannot reduce it with your hands alone, then use this instrument. It is made of iron, with a length of seven or eight digits and a width according to the amount of bleeding. Therefore, it is necessary for the physician to have three or four of these on hand, according to what is needed in all types of fractures."
"And once it is reduced, apply a splint with gentle compression to prevent it from becoming displaced during the operation."
"And if the extremity is sharp and has a border on its sides, then its upper part should be thicker than its lower part, and its middle should have a greater thickness."
"This is how it looks:"
"And it is called in Greek 'Labre Volentz Atelarem', which means roughly 'stone of pain'."
"Why must the end of the limb be raised above the prominence of the bone?"
"And pull it back in such a way that the bone returns to its place, and is covered with some padding."
"And then force the ends of the fracture to come together, and if one end is too thin to grasp with the instrument, it is necessary for you to insert a small instrument between them so that it can grasp onto that bone."
"But if you cannot reduce the bone with what has been recommended here, then cut it with whatever tool you have available among those that we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whichever way seems most feasible to you."
"And also scrape away any roughness and small splinters remaining on the bone."
"And when the bone is reduced and during its reduction the patient feels very severe pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; therefore do this, so that you may give great relief to the patient."
"When your restoration of the bone is complete, then wrap it in a cloth soaked in black stiptic wine, especially if it is summer time, and do not let any oil or other substance come into contact with it, so that putrefaction and corruption may not occur later."
"And also administer bandages at the time of completion of the bone restoration, and leave the dressing uncovered in such a way that you may insert the splint with forceps and use wrappings according to the quantity of the dressing."
"And be very careful that you do not stretch the dressing over the fracture, for many times foolish doctors have done this and caused death or corrupting illness or swelling in their patients, which is not good." "And if your bandage is light, soft, and different from the bandages of other fractures."
"And if the dressing is large or is a bad dressing, and you fear that some of those unfortunate accidents which we have mentioned may occur on it, and you find pain in that place, then do not rely on bandages, but make a wrapping of hard cloths there instead of bandages and stretch it with them."
"And when after one or two days the dressing begins to grow warm in that place, then you remove from there the cloth which you had put on it with wine, and apply cataplasms, such as those by which we usually treat dressings, like a cataplasm of treacle and similar ones."
"And it is advisable for you to loosen the bandage and examine the dressing every day morning and evening until it is consolidated and firm, and the member should be prepared in such a way that putrefaction may flow from there to the parts below with ease."
"If many days pass and the dressing is not consolidated nor separated from the warmth, then you should know that here are the beginnings of gangrene, therefore you must examine the dressing with caution." "And those gangrenous parts which are separated and removed you should cut away, but those which are not separated and cause the member to swell and cause pain, you should pierce according to what is possible for you."
"And if something like chyme or ichor occurs in the dressing, then it is stoimenia or some other species of corruption and putrefaction, therefore you should apply one of those which are mentioned here in its chapter regarding the cure of that."
"And regarding that in which I should speak better and you make your own judgment: when the bone is crushed, do not presume traction of it, nor extraction of it because many times death has occurred because of this; let putrefaction take its course there, for strong flux will come by itself after about 20 or 30 days; and then treat the dressing if you can see it, and if not leave it alone."
"Chapter XX."
"On the treatment of inflammation that occurs in the region of some fracture."
Many times this condition occurs during the healing process of a fracture, especially near joints. It is identified by the distortion of the limb's shape. And it often prevents the limb from its natural operation.
Then consider whether the swelling is recent and administer those medications that have astringent properties like aloevera, ensens, myrrh, and acacia. Use them generously and mix them with either egg albumen, white vinegar or sour wine. Then place this mixture over the swelling wrapped in linen, applying pressure with a firm bandage.
And let the bandage remain in place for several days without removing it; and gradually remove it when the swelling has subsided. Or apply over it a lead plate descended from above because lead has the property to reduce all inflammation in limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned into bone and is causing pain, then it must be cut open carefully, and make an incision along the natural crease of the limb, excising any excess flesh or bony protuberance. Then rinse it with warm water until it is healed. And dress it accordingly until it is completely healed.
Chapter XXI
On the restoration of fractures when they are healed, but if the limb remains weak and does not align naturally
When a bone fracture has healed, but the limb remains weak and crooked, it is usually due to one of several causes: either too much dissolution of the ligaments and tissues around the fracture site so that they do not heal properly; or excess tightening of the ligaments preventing adequate nourishment from reaching the limb; or too much embrocation applied to the area; or excessive movement before the bone has fully healed; or lack of blood circulation in the body due to weakness or debility.
And the treatment for this is to ensure the patient receives proper nutrition in order to increase blood circulation and strengthen their body. Apply warm baths, and massage gently with gentle movements to stimulate healing and promote alignment of the limb.
And apply gentle traction to the limb using a splint in order to draw more nourishment to it. Or continue applying warm compresses with hot water to promote healing and restore alignment to its natural state.
Chapter XXII
"Regarding the treatment of crushed bones when they have been restored crookedly, prohibit their operation in such manner."
"When it happens to a member already restored near its healing time from being twisted and prominent at the crushed bone's nodule, although the member is not fededa in shape but still allows its natural operation, you should not receive the word of anyone who asserts that such member is newly crushed."
"But already many foolish doctors and healers have done this in our land, and this operation is greatly to be blamed for it brings great fear along with its mortal sign."
"But if the limpness and nodule are recent, then they should be wrapped in warm water with softening herbs such as marshmallow roots and rosemary leaves, mallows, and similar ones; and a plaster of mollifying ointment should be made thus with dill well-made, or mucilage of marshmallow root should be pressed with grease of goose or oil of cypress and applied therewith; or fatty figs should be pressed with pigeon's dung and similar to those medicines called amulets for consolidation."
"And sometimes they are dissolved with frequent gentle friction that should be done with the moistest ones, and then the member should be moved in all parts at every hour."
"But if the twistedness is already advanced and has become hard and converted into bone, and there is necessity for its cure with iron, then it should be cut away from above and the continuity of the bone should be dissolved, and what is superfluous of the nodule of that member with the bone should be cut away with subtle scissors, and facilitation should be administered in that one with care and study."
Chapter XXIII
Of speech concerning dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of some of the joints of the body, which causes restricted movement and deformity of the limb, resulting in great pain and injury.
Therefore when some dislocation occurs, it is necessary to promptly reduce it, and not delay its reduction, for if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and hard, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And therefore it should not be moved nor stretched at the time of inflammation, for many times it causes spasm and intense pain.
But when this occurs, it is necessary that you quickly apply a poultice to the affected area and allow it to rest until the inflammation has subsided. Then the limb should be wrapped with hot water and oil, and gently reduced.
And all members should be treated with that of which the memory comes into its place.
And I have already arranged the chapters on dislocation according to what has been said about fractures, from the most elevated part of the body down to the most inferior.
Chapter XXIV.
"Treatment of dislocation of the jaw."
"If both jaws are dislocated, they must be reduced; the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways: either they have been moved slightly from their sockets and softened a little; or they have been completely dislocated, so that they are mollified at the point of articulation, when saliva flows from the patient's mouth and he cannot open nor close his mouth, with his tongue wrapped in his cheek."
"When the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its place on its own without any cause."
"If the dislocation is complete and full, it is necessary that reduction be administered gently, and not delayed at all."
"And let the minister hold the patient's head, and insert his thumb or both thumbs into the jaw joint inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side; or that he insert both thumbs together if the dislocation is on both sides, and keep the other fingers outside with which he engages; and command the patient to close his jaws and stop them from moving from each part; and the doctor gently pushes the jaw back into its place."
"And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated at once, then evaporation with hot water and oil should be administered, so that the reduction may be made more easily and not delayed at all, as we have said."
"And then when the reduction has been made and the jaws have been closed, and the patient's mouth is not softened, powdered cloth with wax should be placed over it, which is made of wax and rose oil; and let it be tied gently with soft linen."
"And let the patient sleep on his back, and let his head be supported between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right or left; and do not force him to swallow anything at first; but let his food be liquid gruel, until when the pains have subsided and the jaws are healed, then let him eat what he wishes."
"And this should be administered gently, and not worked upon the opening of the mouth during eating and drinking and laughing, until the jaw is healed."
"And if the reduction of two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and do not return to their places, then often vomiting occurs with fever and continuous thirst, and sometimes the abdomen of the patient becomes swollen, and there may also be pure colic."
"And when this happens, then know that it is a fatal condition; and often the patient dies within ten days."
"Chapter XXV."
"On the reduction of dislocation of the clavicle and its extremity."
"The furcula is not dislocated from the inside due to its connection with the chest wall; however, sometimes it may be dislocated externally, which can be felt."
"The restoration of it is that the patient lies on their back and extends their arms." Then press the area with your palm using firm pressure, for it will return.
Then pause over that plaster and powders and strengthen that.
If the end of that which seizes the vein does not continue with it, then it is not displaced in any way; therefore it is necessary to reduce and align it according to what we have said and according to what you have prepared.
Then pause over that plaster, powders, and bandage, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until they are healed.
With this treatment, the end of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter xxvi
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated at the beginning of the titillation on the left side.
And the second is that it is dislocated at the beginning above the muscle, and that is rare; and it is not dislocated at the back parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front parts by the nerves.
And mostly it is dislocated and exits at the left parts above the titillation, and more easily in those of whom the flesh is little, but in those with much flesh, it exits out with difficulty.
And sometimes, especially for some men struck by blow or fall, the muscle becomes inflamed with an incurable abscess, and it is crushed by that which is already dislocated.
Therefore, you should proceed in this manner until you are sure about it, and afterwards proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation of it is known when it is at the left parts above the titillation; for this reason you can distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the sound one, because there is a manifest diversity between them. You find in the head of the muscle a concavity on the left side of the titillation, and you touch the head of the muscle like this, as if it were a goose egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move that at all by any movement.
If it is dislocated below or above, and you afterwards find a manifest touch, it will not be hidden.
And from this dislocation, easy reduction is made when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
"To reduce it, the minister raises the patient's hand above their head. Then place your feet on either side of the patient's genitals. Next, raise the joint with strength to its original position, and then the minister raises the patient's arm and extends it upwards."
"If it does not return (the limb), then, from the beginning when the dislocation occurred, several days or even hours may have passed. Therefore, the patient should be bathed in warm water and rubbed with an ointment that is moisturizing and soothing, such as one made from ground aloe vera, fenugreek seeds, and marsh mallow root in water."
"And then the patient should be propped up on their back and placed on either side of the testicles waiting for it to regain its normal position between elevated and constricted. And then the doctor places his foot on the sphere (a ball or globe) and lifts the head of the muscle with force, pulling the patient's arm so that it is not moved from its position. Another assistant should hold the patient's head so that it is not moved from its place as the limb soon returns to its proper location."
"And if you wish, you can reduce it in this manner: have a man present who is taller than the patient. He should stand on the side of the limb that is longer and insert his muscle between the patient's testicles and elevate them upwards while the patient is suspended in air. Another assistant should pull the patient's arm down towards their belly." "But if the patient is lighter, it is necessary that they are suspended by something else (besides their arm), otherwise the dislocation will quickly return."
"And it can be reduced in the same way: place on the ground a long pole with a round head like a pestle that is not too thick or thin. Then place the patient on top of it. Cover the top of the pole with soft cloths and have the patient stand along the length of the pole, stretching their arm out to the other side. Stretch the body on the other side with force because the joint will quickly return to its proper location."
"And if the reduction is difficult due to all the causes mentioned above, then use this treatment: take a pole that is two arms' length long and four fingers wide. The thickness of it should be equal to the length of the thumb and place it between the testicles in this way:"
"And then place soft cloths on top of the round head of the pole so that it does not hurt the patient."
"And then place the pole on top of the patient's testicles. Extend their arm or leg over the pole towards the other side and bind the pole to the assistant's arms and the end of the extended limb."
"And then place the arm on a ladder according to its width. Extend the hand towards the other side and suspend the other body on the other side because the joint will soon enter."
"And when the joint is fully reduced according to any possible method, then place a pad made of wool of equal quantity over the testicles. Then apply a plaster prepared from powdered millet with honey and egg whites all over the muscle as it is turned upwards. Then bind it decently around the testicles. Wrap the bandage over the plaster and tie the patient's hand to their neck, leaving it untied for seven days without moving their arm."
"And the patient should be given little food until the limb is strengthened because it will then be easier to heal."
"And then give more food after seven or eight days and have the limb exercised. And when it is firm and not softened, then it is healed."
"But if the joint is frequently dislocated due to humidity that occurs in it or for another cause, then cauterization should be administered with three incisions as mentioned earlier in the chapter on cautery."
"And even if all of that is done and after seven days the bandage is removed but the joint is not firm, and the plaster and binding are reapplied several times but it is still not firm and caught and cannot raise that arm upwards, then know that the nerves at the head of the muscle have been injured or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be put back in its proper place."
"Regarding the dislocation of that [the elbow], it occurs in the pit of the arm and the nipple or behind the shoulder blade. The reduction is done with pressure and extension using the hands until it returns."
"And apply another bandage to it [the elbow] and treat it until it heals."
"And if gravity occurs in the limb and slowness of movement during its healing, then the patient should use baths frequently until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original nature."
"Chapter XXVII."
"On the treatment of dislocation of the hip."
"You should know that the hip joint is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; and one that is dislocated cannot be reduced without great pain, especially if it is dislocated in front or behind. The dislocation of the hip is not noticeable until the patient tries to walk or touch their knee according to which position it was dislocated in, and when you reduce the dislocated hip joint with its socket, that will be made clear to you manifestly, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the leg nor touch one's own muscle."
"And you should reduce it and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before an abscess forms; for if an abscess forms, the reduction becomes difficult, and often it is not healed completely. And especially when it is dislocated in back positions, because among all other species of dislocation this one is the worst, causing much pain, and many times death ensues from that."
"And the reduction of it, when it is possible to reduce it, consists in extending the limb with two hands and abducting the leg; then place your hands on the hip joint and push it back into its socket with the pressure of both palms together or with the palm of your hand turned inward so that it returns to its proper position."
"But if the dislocation is in front positions, then return it thus by flexing the limb with your hand upon it until it impacts the hip joint with the palm of your hand."
"And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then apply vigorous extension and great force."
"And it is that two assistants should extend the limb, and hold it firmly in place with two hands so that it is not removed during extension."
"And then it should be rotated towards all parts because a long folded bandage is wrapped around the hand of the limb or the limb itself."
"And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he should grease it with some oil so that it may facilitate those causes which help in the lubricity of the joint. And then push the joint back vigorously until it returns."
"And then the reduction of it should be kept upon that support where there is stability and elevation with album of wool, and it should be extended with a descent suspension and the limb suspended from the neck for some days."
"And then let it be untied; but if the joint is formed in its proper place, then you may untie the bandage from it and leave it alone."
"But if you see that the joint is not strong, then apply the compress and bandage again and leave them on for some days until it is extended. And then untie it."
"But if gravity is against the reduction of the joint and the position of the limb is in motion, then apply moistening with bath and gentle friction and pressure until it is alleviated. Or place it on elevated support so that it may be a thing of moving fat. And then bind it and leave it alone over the bone day and night." "And then remove that and put him in a bath; and when he is warm, then rub the joint with gentle friction, and then place it on elevated support so that it may be a thing of motion once again, twice, thrice." "and repeatedly bathe it until it becomes limber."
"And if you wish, place it on a moistened and scraped cowhide fold and press upon it. And do this several times until that becomes moistened and reduces to its proper position."
"Chapter XXVIII"
Of the treatment of dislocation of the wrist, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.
"Sometimes the wrist is dislocated and its reduction is easy, whereas the opposite is true for other joints. However, care should be taken to reduce it quickly in the moment that it occurs, before inflammation sets in or any swelling forms."
"And the reduction of the dislocation is that you place the wrist of the patient on a table and an assistant extends the hand of the patient. Then the physician places his hand or palm over the prominence of the joint and presses it until it returns to its proper position."
"But it is important to consider whether the palm has already been dislocated onto the front of the hand. If it has, then the patient should place the back of their hand on a table for extension and reduction. But if the dislocation is anterior to the back of the hand, then the position of the patient's hand should be intricately placed on the table so that the palm of the physician may fit over the prominence of the joint."
"And then if it returns immediately, that is good. But if not, then apply pressure with a compress to reduce the swelling and leave it alone. And do not repeat this, because if not done carefully, you will not be able to succeed in reducing it after some days have passed."
"But the joint becomes stiff according to its tortuousness; and do not inform the patient of any cause, but simply tell him that his hand is softened and cannot strengthen for any reason. Then you know that the nerves are compressed or torn, and then there will be no improvement in it unless it is strengthened with caustic applied near its attachment and rubbed gently with some cause."
"So when the dislocation has returned, then apply the compress which we have recommended and leave it on for five days. And then untie it and let it rest and exercise the hand of it."
"But if the motion of that is lost and there is some gravity in it, then lenify it with cold water and gentle friction several times until it is alleviated."
"Chapter XXIX"
Of the treatment of dislocation of the toes.
Sometimes the toes are completely dislocated.
"And when one of those toes is dislocated to the back or front of it, then grasp that toe and press upon its dislocation with your thumbs until it returns."
"And then support the head of the toe, suspend it over the part where it is dislocated and leave it for two days."
"And then suspend that and extend it until the day is finished."
"And when night comes, bind that according to this description and do not cease loosening it in the day and exercising it with motion. And bind it at night for several days until it is straight."
"And do the same in this way if it is dislocated to the front of the hand, and bind it at that angle and do your first operation with it until it is healed."
"And similarly do this when it is dislocated to other parts."
"Chapter XXX"
Of the treatment of dislocation of the spinal vertebrae.
"When complete dislocation occurs in any of the spinal vertebrae or those of the neck, which are removed from their proper positions, then there is no treatment for that because death will overtake the patient."
"And the sign of that is that the evacuation of the patient occurs involuntarily without his being able to retain it. And many times some members of him or both legs of him or both arms of him are paralyzed, or one of those. Or else one vertebra has been removed from its position because often times such removal has occurred and likewise the dislocation of them has happened in all four directions."
"So that is already removed to the posterior direction and is called paralysis."
"But the treatment of it is that you should then consider whether the paralysis has occurred from birth because in that case there is neither treatment nor healing for it."
"However, in the manner that it comes by chance or by percussion or similarly, as was explained by the Ancients with lengthy speeches, which return mostly in vain; yet I have abbreviated that of which little excused the excess of it, due to my proximity to my intended exposition, with which this has been explained and formed from diverse instruments."
"And among these, only that one is cured which occurs properly in the back, with what I recount here."
"And it is that if the disease extends over its surface near a wall, and is extended from below up to where the tool touches it, then the stone will not be harmed."
"And properly a straight beam should be placed, set on the ground at the end of the stone near its head, and another beam laid across its feet at the other end of the stone."
"And the servant should hold the beam, and not be held in a hole; and another servant should hold the other one according to that form."
"And properly wrap the head of the stone and below its titlings firmly with a rope; extend the end of the rope to the beam that is above the head of it, and tie it there; and also stretch the other rope over the haunches of the stone on both sides of it, and place it under the curves of it; and properly adjust all the ropes and tie them to the other beam that is above the feet of it."
"And properly each servant should extend the rope at the departure of the beam, and the two beams and ropes of their places in which they are set should not be moved, but nevertheless not fastened as we have said."
"And the doctor should place his palms on the stone with firmness while it returns, or if he places them on that one table, then properly adjusts his feet on the table while it returns."
"But if it does not return by this method, then take a plank whose width or length is about three brazas, and dig in the wall that we have said is near the stone a place in which the end of the plank can be inserted." "And properly place the middle of the plank on the stone, and the doctor places his weight on the other end, and stretches well so that the stone is constricted and returns to its place."
"And if you wish, turn the stone that has revolved with your hand; and if you place yourself on the ground near the head of the stone in the rear of the two beams, the length of which is one braza, and similarly place yourself at the head of it behind the two beams; and let the distance between each beam be a palm, and already each beam is drawn in the one that returns, and each should be firmly fixed on the ground so that it is not moved."
"And let there also be one round beam on which the stone turns, and let it be turned in the two tracks of the two beams."
"And at its end is a track in which the beam is fitted, the length of which is a palm and a half when it is turned; and the other two beams are similar to this one."
"And properly stretch the ropes that you have stretched in the stone of the stone at the track that is above its head, and the ropes that you have stretched on its sides at the turn that is under its feet."
"And properly place one servant near each turn who twists the beam with his hand, and the doctor guides the stone as we have said."
"And then when the stone has returned and the place is cleared, then you should put plaster with album of egg white and also cotton; and properly place it on the plaster as on a table whose width is about three fingers or somewhat more, and its length is the quantity that takes the place of the concavity, and at the departure of the stone."
"And properly let it be bound with the bandage that we have mentioned, and use the stone for nourishment until it is healed."
"And if there remains some tenderness in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and clean with administration of the table for a long time, which we have recommended; and already a lead plate is administered in that place."
"And sometimes tenderness occurs in the back of the two buttocks and it is cured by that dislocation; and this bone already inclines towards breaking because it should not be opened with this medicine, for strong labor will cause you to die."
"Chapter LXXXI."
"Of the cure of a dislocated hip."
"You should know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle only endures dislocation alone, and it does not happen to these as it happens to other joints with little movement and concavity."
"And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways: because it is dislocated at its inner parts, or at its outer parts, or at its front parts, or at its rear parts."
"And more often it is dislocated at its inner parts, and less so at its front parts or its rear parts."
"The sign of dislocation at its inner parts is that when you press the healthy buttock against the sick one, it becomes longer and the anus appears more prominent than in the healthy one; and the sick one cannot bend his knee downwards, and the place where the knee touches is hot with red inflammation due to a bruise, because the head of the bone has already come here."
"The sign for that one in whom dislocation occurs at its outer parts is that his accidents are contrary to this accident."
"The sign for that one in whom dislocation occurs at its front parts is that he extends his buttock completely but does not bend it without pain that is in the anus; and if he tries to walk he cannot do so at the front parts; and his urine is retained and his abdomen is inflamed; and his walking is done on tiptoe."
"The sign for that one in whom dislocation occurs at its rear parts is that he does not extend his anus and cannot bend it forwards, but only backwards; and his buttock appears shorter than the other one, and the abdomen is softened; and the head of the bone is near that place."
"But the reduction of the kinds of this dislocation is that you should consider whether the dislocation is old and ancient, which has already happened with its being in such a state; then you administer reduction according to its disposition, but if it is not in that cure; and therefore no opening should be made at that time."
"But in that one whose dislocation is recent and is one of the four kinds of dislocation, you should strive to return the joint, extend it at its inner parts and outer parts, and move it to the right and left; because strong labor returns it and there is no need for another cure."
"But if it does not return in this manner, then you should prepare a strong lever and extend the buttock of that side, or with its mass, or with a band which is tied above the anus; and another assistant extends his hand over it in such a way that he places his hands behind the thigh; and you yourself pull strongly with a band over the bone of the thing; and another assistant holds the end of the band."
"The extension of his or her leg from within to the angle at the shoulder blade, or from behind to the point of the elbow."
"And all this should be done in one go while the patient is lying on their back with their head hanging down."
"And this type of extension is specific to each of the four."
"If the dislocation returns as we have said, it will be good. Otherwise, now is necessary for each type of this what to say about its own treatment."
"In the reduction of the leg itself, when it is dislocated and the dislocation is on the inner side, the patient should be made to sit up on their left side; then apply pressure on the ligaments over the ridge of the hip in that place where between the head of the hip and the place that is next to the angle of the shoulder blade."
"And then another should strongly seize the leg of him or her, and firmly hold the thick part of the diseased hip, extending it forcibly outward, for that will return to its place."
"And this method is one of easier treatment whereby this member is reduced than the other methods."
"But if this does not succeed at once and you do not obey the entrance with this method of treatment for all, then both feet of the patient should be lifted together with a strong, light lien over both ankles and calves."
"And let the length of each be about four fingers, and let the limb of the patient be extended more than the other by two fingers."
"And then the patient should be suspended from the top of the pillar and let him or her hang downwards, with his or her leg hanging down to the ground about two cubits."
"And then command one strong youth to seize the head of the hip, and let him or her hang together with the patient; and another youth push the hip with great force, for the joint will return to its place with speed."
"But in the reduction itself, when there is dislocation on the outer side, the patient should lie down on his or her back according to what we have recommended in the case of gibbosity; and let the lien be stretched over the diseased limb properly and over the foot of it."
"And then two pillars should be placed, one at the foot and the other at the head; and another pillar should be fitted into the middle of the gap, covered with damp cloths, so that the patient may not be injured by the pillar being between his or her thighs, for thus the extension to the joints will not be hindered."
"And then each minister should extend his or her part, and the doctor with his hand equalize the reduction according to the art."
"But when the dislocation is on the front parts, then the limb should be extended lightly, and this is according to the method described above on the elbow; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the knee of the patient, and soon after catches that with the other hand, and with that catching extends it at the joints to the ankle."
"But if the dislocation is on the back parts, then the patient need not be extended at the joints, but should be elevated from the ground. Then it will be necessary to place him or her on a hard couch, as it would be in the case of a dislocated shoulder according to what we have said about the setting up of two shoulders; and he or she lies on his or her face with the lien extended thus."
"And compression should be administered with a board over the place where the joint is located."
Therefore, once the reduction is completed as it should be - and the sign of the reduction of that member is not hidden from you, which is that you stretch both thighs of the patient, and when you do those movements and the patient extends their thigh and expands that side without excuse, then you know that the member has been returned as it should; then you adjust both sides and place a compress and extend with a bandage so that nothing be hidden in any of the parts; and have the patient rest for three days or four.
And afterwards release the ligament and the compress, and compare one thigh with the other.
Then if you see those movements in truth, then you know that the dislocation is already fixed, and at that point let the patient go.
And if you see any cause of softening, then return and plaster that member again, and stretch it according to your first method, and leave it for three days.
And afterwards release that member, and have the patient walk on crutches for a few days until they are strengthened.
Chapter XXXII
On the treatment of dislocation of the knee joint
The knee joint is dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated at the outside parts, inside parts, and behind parts; but it cannot be dislocated at the front parts.
The sign of dislocation of it is that when you command the patient to apply their thigh against their shinbone, then you know that the knee joint is dislocated.
And the reduction of all kinds of it is that you make the patient lie on their right side, so that they can extend their thigh if possible; then place your two hands against the joint, and place the sole of your foot between their feet; next press the knee joint with the palms of your hands and hold it there firmly with both your hands while another assistant extends the patient's leg until the knee joint returns to its proper position.
The sign of reduction of it is that when the thigh adheres to the shinbone with ease and without labor.
And afterwards plaster it and have the thigh adhere to the shinbone; next bind those together with a bandage for three or four days, and then release them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days until they are healed and strengthened.
But if reduction of it cannot be done by you, then apply strong extension with splints, as in the treatment of dislocation of the hip joint previously described.
Chapter XXXIII.
"Of the treatment of ankle dislocation."
"Sometimes the ankle is merely sprained by minor trauma, and sometimes it becomes dislocated completely, in which case the dislocation occurs either inward or outward."
"And the sign of ankle dislocation is that sometimes the ankle swells at the joint from which it has become dislocated."
"The treatment of an ankle sprain is relatively easy: the foot should be extended with ease and its movements restricted until it returns."
"However, in the treatment of a completely dislocated ankle, you must firmly grasp the patient's foot and have a strong servant hold the joint behind them at the midpoint of the leg."
"And place your right hand with your thumb above the top of the foot and your left hand below it at the level of the heel."
"And pull the foot towards you with your right hand while pressing twice with your left hand, and then extend it with your left hand, pushing the sole of the foot up with your right hand without twisting."
"And perform this maneuver twice as we have described."
"And finally push the sole of the foot up on the third attempt, and you will be at the heel."
If this procedure is repeated once or twice more, and if the foot becomes swollen, then that's good. Otherwise, repeat the operation because that one has returned.
But if you are prohibited from reducing the hernia with what has been mentioned above, then have the patient lie on their side on the ground, and place a small stone between their thighs, ensuring it is firmly placed. Then quickly cover them with cloth so that the patient does not slip.
And the minister of the thighs should hold them in place, and another minister should extend the leg with their hands or some linen placed on the top of the thigh. And each minister should stand in such a way that they can fully extend their side, and the small stone should be held between the patient's thighs to prevent the body from being pulled towards its sides during the extension.
"The doctor then reduces the dislocation with his hands, while another attendant holds the healthy limb in place, for the dislocation returns easily."
"And once the dislocation has returned and declared to be in good health from its reduction, plaster is then applied and strengthened with bandages, and the foot is tied to the bed."
"And be careful or guard against touching the nerves which are behind the heel bone, for they not press on it too vigorously."
"And then leave that for two or three days. And if the bandage becomes moistened, change it."
"Then remove the splint on the third or fourth day, and prohibit walking for forty days." "But if it is necessary to walk during that period, there is danger that the dislocation will not remain firm and may be corrupted, so that it will receive no cure."
"And if an ulcer should form from the cautery, then you should apply to it the same treatment as described earlier in this narrative, until it is healed."
Chapter XXXIV.
"On the Treatment of Dislocations of the Ankles"
"The one who has been dislocated should be gently stretched without struggle."
"If the dislocation is in some bones of the lower back, you should then make the patient lie on their stomach on the ground or on a table."
"And then place your hands on the patient's back at the joint area and press down with force until it pops back into place. Ensure that it has been repositioned correctly."
"Then place the patient's stomach on a table that can support their entire weight. Place two pillows under them and keep them immobilized with a firm but gentle stretch for three days. Then remove the restraints and allow the patient to rest from walking for a few more days until they have regained strength."
Capítulo XXXV
"Chapter XXXV. Of the different types of dislocation that occur with fracture or breakage, or both at once."
"When one of those causes occurs and you attempt to treat it and restore the patient, many times death will ensue."
"And therefore one should not presume to undertake the treatment of such cases, but only someone who is skilled in the art, experienced, compassionate and merciful, careful, not hasty or arrogant. And at the beginning of treatment, one should use medicines that only draw out corrupted humors, and leave the patient to the divine judgment, hoping for recovery rather than perdition, with alleviation of the illness and some hope of success." "And therefore, as soon as possible after the onset of the cause, make an effort to reduce the dislocation before a corrupted ulcer forms."
"And if the member returns as desired, then apply treatment that will draw out corrupted humors and heal the wound with suitable remedies for dried-out flesh."
"And if dislocation occurs with fracture and the bone fragments are separated, then attempt to reduce them, following what has been said previously about simple fractures in those locations."
"And guard and avoid entering the paths of fear according to your ability, as I have previously commanded you; for this is better for you than anything else that you owe to your Creator, more enduring to your glory, and healthier for your true self in your time." "So endeth the words of Albucasis, may God bless them." "Amen."
